Similarly for Γ: N=1, |A + t v - 1|^2 = R^2. Since |A-1|^2 = R^2. Then |A-1 + t v|^2 = R^2 => |A-1|^2 + 2t (A-1)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 => 2t (A-1)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t(2 (A-1)·v + t |v|^2)=0 => t=0 gives A; other t = t_F = -2 (A-1)·v / |v|^2.

So we have explicit t_E and t_F.

Let's attempt a synthetic approach.

We have two circles Ω and Γ with centers M,N intersecting at A,B. Let C and D be the points on MN such that C on Ω, D on Γ, and order C-M-N-D. So C and D are on opposite sides of the segment MN? Actually M lies between C and N, N between M and D, so C is on the ray from M away from N, D on ray from N away from M.

Define P = circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A is on both circles, and C on Ω, D on Γ, we have that M lies on perpendicular bisector of AC, N on perpendicular bisector of AD, and the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical (if MN horizontal). So P is the intersection of lines through M perpendicular to AC and through N perpendicular to AD. So we can say that PM ⟂ AC and PN ⟂ AD. So ∠(PM, AC) = 90°, ∠(PN, AD)=90°.

Now consider the circle with diameter MN? Or the circle through M,N,P? Since P is defined by perpendiculars, we can look at triangle PMN. Its orthocenter H. Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN, so it's vertical. So H lies on that vertical line. Also, altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, and from N perpendicular to PM. So H is the intersection of these altitudes.

Now line AP meets Ω again at E, and Γ again at F. So A, E, P, F collinear. Also, note that A, C, M are on Ω; A, D, N on Γ. So we have cyclic quadrilaterals? Actually A, C, M are not necessarily cyclic? They are on Ω, so A, C, M are on the circle Ω (M is center, not on circle). So A, C are on Ω, M is center, so not on circle. So cyclic quadrilaterals: A, C, B? Not.

Perhaps we can use the fact that the circumcircle of triangle BEF is the reflection of the circumcircle of triangle AEF? Since B is the reflection of A across MN, and E,F lie on line AP, which is not symmetric. But if we reflect the whole configuration across MN, then Ω and Γ are symmetric? Actually reflection across MN (the line of centers) sends Ω to itself? Not necessarily; Ω is centered at M on MN, so reflection across MN (the line containing M,N) sends Ω to itself because the circle is symmetric about any line through its center. So we can solve.

So from H = (X, y_P - 2y_O), we have y_P - 2y_O = y_H. So y_O = (y_P - y_H)/2 = (y_P - X(1-X)/y_P)/2. That could be used.

Now, the line through H parallel to AP: direction v = P-A. So L: H + s v. We need to show that L is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. Now B = conjugate of A (since A and B symmetric about real axis). So B = \overline{A}. Also, E and F are on line AP, which is the line through A and P. So they are also on the same line. So points A, P, E, F collinear. Since A and B are symmetric about real axis, and the line AP is not necessarily symmetric, but its reflection across real axis is line B P', where P' is reflection of P across real axis. So triangle B E' F' is the reflection of triangle AEF. But triangle BEF is not the reflection of AEF unless E and F are symmetric? Actually, if we reflect triangle AEF across real axis, we get triangle B E' F'. So triangle BEF is different from that reflection unless E=F'? Not.

But note that B, E, F are all on the same side of real axis? In numeric, B y negative, E y negative, F y negative. So all below axis. So the circumcircle of BEF lies mostly below axis. The line L passes through H, which is also below axis (y_H negative). So L is a line through H.

Maybe we can prove that the circumcircle of BEF is orthogonal to the circle with diameter? Or that the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the line through H? Not.

Another approach: Use inversion about A with radius such that the circles Ω and Γ become lines. Then E and F become intersections of those lines with line AP (which is invariant). Maybe we can use the fact that the orthocenter of triangle PMN is the center of the circle through the reflections of P across M and N? Not.

Now, (a^2 - b^2) = (a-b)(a+b). a = sin(θ/2)-cosθ, b = sin(θ/2)+1. So a-b = -cosθ -1 = -(1+cosθ) = -2 cos^2(θ/2). a+b = (sin(θ/2)-cosθ)+(sin(θ/2)+1)= 2 sin(θ/2) - cosθ +1. And we already computed -cosθ+1 = 2 sin^2(θ/2). So a+b = 2 sin(θ/2) + 2 sin^2(θ/2) = 2 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)). So (a^2-b^2) = (-2 cos^2(θ/2)) * 2 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) = -4 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) cos^2(θ/2). Then equation: -4 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) cos^2(θ/2) -2y sinθ + sin^2θ = 0 => 2y sinθ = sin^2θ - 4 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) cos^2(θ/2). Now sinθ = 2 sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2). So left: 2y * 2 sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) = 4y sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2). So:

4y sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) = sin^2θ - 4 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) cos^2(θ/2). But sin^2θ = 4 sin^2(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2). So:

4y sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) = 4 sin^2(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2) - 4 sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) cos^2(θ/2). Factor 4 cos^2(θ/2): = 4 cos^2(θ/2)[ sin^2(θ/2) - sin(θ/2)(1+ sin(θ/2)) ] = 4 cos^2(θ/2)[ sin^2(θ/2) - sin(θ/2) - sin^2(θ/2)] = 4 cos^2(θ/2)[ - sin(θ/2)] = -4 sin(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2). So:

4y sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) = -4 sin(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2). Cancel 4 sin(θ/2) (nonzero): y cos(θ/2) = - cos^2(θ/2) => y = - cos(θ/2). So P = (sin(θ/2), -cos(θ/2)). That is a nice result! Now we have B = (1-2s^2, -2s c). P = (s, -c). F = (1-2s+4s^3, -4s^2 c). All points have y negative (since c>0, s>0.5, -2s c negative, -c negative, -4s^2 c negative). So triangle B P F is below x-axis.

Now we need H = orthocenter of triangle PMN. With M=(0,0), N=(1,0), P=(s, -c). We already have H = (s, y_H) with y_H = - sin(θ/2)(1-sin(θ/2))/cos(θ/2) = - s (1-s)/c? Actually from earlier: H = (s, - s(1-s)/c). Let's compute: y_H = - sin(θ/2)(1- sin(θ/2))/cos(θ/2) = - s (1-s)/c. Yes.

Now line through H parallel to AP: direction v = P-A = (2s^2+s-1, -c(1+2s)) = ((2s-1)(s+1), -c(1+2s)). So L: H + t v, t real.

We need to show that L is tangent to circumcircle of triangle B P F.

Now we can compute the circumcircle of B P F. Since we have coordinates, we can find its center O and radius R_circ. Then verify that distance from O to L equals radius. This is an algebraic condition in s and c (with c^2=1-s^2). It should simplify to an identity for s in (0.5,1). This is plausible.

Let's attempt to compute O. Points:

B = (1-2s^2, -2s c)
P = (s, -c)
F = (1-2s+4s^3, -4s^2 c)

We can use the fact that these three points are not collinear. We can find the circumcenter by solving the perpendicular bisector equations. Since we have symmetry? Not exactly.

We can compute using complex numbers: Let’s treat points as complex numbers. B = 1-2s^2 - 2i s c, P = s - i c, F = 1-2s+4s^3 - i 4s^2 c. But note that these points are on the same line? No.

Alternatively, we can compute the circle equation: |z - O|^2 = R^2. We need O such that distances to B,P,F equal. We can find O as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Since we have coordinates, we can set up linear equations.

Let O = (Ox, Oy). Then:

|O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2.

From |O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2: (Ox - (1-2s^2))^2 + (Oy - (-2s c))^2 = (Ox - s)^2 + (Oy - (-c))^2. Simplify: (Ox - (1-2s^2))^2 + (Oy + 2s c)^2 = (Ox - s)^2 + (Oy + c)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2Ox(1-2s^2) + (1-2s^2)^2 + Oy^2 +4s c Oy +4s^2 c^2 = Ox^2 -2s Ox + s^2 + Oy^2 +2c Oy + c^2. Cancel Ox^2,Oy^2:

-2Ox(1-2s^2) + (1-2s^2)^2 +4s c Oy +4s^2 c^2 = -2s Ox + s^2 +2c Oy + c^2.

Bring terms:

-2Ox(1-2s^2) +2s Ox +4s c Oy -2c Oy + (1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2 =0.

Compute coefficient for Ox: -2(1-2s^2)+2s = -2+4s^2+2s = 2s+4s^2-2 = 2(s+2s^2-1). Coefficient for Oy: 4s c -2c = 2c(2s-1). So:

2(s+2s^2-1) Ox + 2c(2s-1) Oy + [(1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2] =0. Divide by 2: (s+2s^2-1) Ox + c(2s-1) Oy + 1/2[(1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2] =0.

Now compute the constant term K = (1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2. Expand (1-2s^2)^2 = 1 -4s^2+4s^4. So K = 1 -4s^2+4s^4 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2 = 1 -5s^2+4s^4 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2. But c^2 = 1-s^2. So -c^2 = -(1-s^2)= -1+s^2. Then K = 1 -5s^2+4s^4 -1+s^2 +4s^2(1-s^2) = (1-1) + (-5s^2+s^2)= -4s^2 +4s^4 +4s^2(1-s^2)= -4s^2+4s^4+4s^2-4s^4=0. So K=0! So the constant term is zero. So equation reduces to: (s+2s^2-1) Ox + c(2s-1) Oy = 0. (Equation (1))

Now from |O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2: (Ox - s)^2 + (Oy + c)^2 = (Ox - (1-2s+4s^3))^2 + (Oy + 4s^2 c)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2s Ox + s^2 + Oy^2 +2c Oy + c^2 = Ox^2 -2Ox(1-2s+4s^3) + (1-2s+4s^3)^2 + Oy^2 +2*4s^2 c Oy + (4s^2 c)^2? Actually (Oy + 4s^2 c)^2 = Oy^2 + 8s^2 c Oy + 16s^4 c^2. So cancel Ox^2,Oy^2:

-2s Ox + s^2 +2c Oy + c^2 = -2(1-2s+4s^3) Ox + (1-2s+4s^3)^2 + 8s^2 c Oy + 16s^4 c^2.

Bring terms:

-2s Ox +2(1-2s+4s^3) Ox +2c Oy -8s^2 c Oy + s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2 =0.

Compute coefficient for Ox: -2s +2(1-2s+4s^3) = -2s +2 -4s+8s^3 = 2 -6s +8s^3 = 2(1 -3s +4s^3). Coefficient for Oy: 2c -8s^2 c = 2c(1-4s^2). So equation:

2(1-3s+4s^3) Ox + 2c(1-4s^2) Oy + (s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2) =0. Divide by 2: (1-3s+4s^3) Ox + c(1-4s^2) Oy + 1/2(s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2)=0.

Now compute constant term L = s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2. Since c^2=1-s^2, then s^2 + c^2 = s^2 + 1 - s^2 =1. So L = 1 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2. Compute (1-2s+4s^3)^2. Let’s denote T = 1-2s+4s^3. Compute T^2 = (1)^2 + (-2s)^2 + (4s^3)^2 + 2*1*(-2s) + 2*1*(4s^3) + 2*(-2s)*(4s^3) = 1 +4s^2+16s^6 -4s +8s^3 -16s^4? Wait: 2*(1)*(-2s) = -4s; 2*(1)*(4s^3)=8s^3; 2*(-2s)*(4s^3)= -16s^4. So T^2 = 1 -4s +4s^2 +8s^3 -16s^4 +16s^6. Then L = 1 - T^2 -16s^4(1-s^2) = 1 - (1 -4s +4s^2 +8s^3 -16s^4 +16s^6) -16s^4 +16s^6 = 1 -1 +4s -4s^2 -8s^3 +16s^4 -16s^6 -16s^4 +16s^6 = (1-1) +4s -4s^2 -8s^3 + (16s^4-16s^4) + (-16s^6+16s^6)= 4s -4s^2 -8s^3 = 4s(1 - s - 2s^2). So L = 4s(1-s-2s^2). Then 1/2 L = 2s(1-s-2s^2). So equation becomes:

(1-3s+4s^3) Ox + c(1-4s^2) Oy + 2s(1-s-2s^2) =0. (Equation (2))

Now we have two linear equations for Ox, Oy. We can solve for Ox and Oy. But note that we also have equation (1): (s+2s^2-1) Ox + c(2s-1) Oy = 0. And we can solve.

Let’s denote:

Eq1: A1 Ox + B1 Oy = 0, where A1 = s+2s^2-1, B1 = c(2s-1).
Eq2: A2 Ox + B2 Oy = -C2, where A2 = 1-3s+4s^3, B2 = c(1-4s^2), C2 = 2s(1-s-2s^2) (positive? For s>0.5, 1-s-2s^2 is negative, so C2 negative? Actually 1-s-2s^2 is negative, so 2s(1-s-2s^2) is negative. So -C2 is positive? We'll keep as is.)

We need to find O = (Ox, Oy). Solve.

From Eq1: Ox = - (B1/A1) Oy, provided A1≠0. For s>0.5, compute A1 = s+2s^2-1. At s=0.5, A1=0.5+0.5-1=0. For s>0.5, A1>0? At s=0.6, 0.6+0.72-1=0.32>0. So A1 positive. So we can write Ox = - (c(2s-1)/(s+2s^2-1)) Oy.

Plug into Eq2: A2 * (- (c(2s-1)/(s+2s^2-1)) Oy) + B2 Oy = -C2 => Oy [ -A2 c(2s-1)/(s+2s^2-1) + B2 ] = -C2.

Now B2 = c(1-4s^2). So factor c: Oy c [ -A2 (2s-1)/(s+2s^2-1) + (1-4s^2) ] = -C2.

Now, A2 = 1-3s+4s^3. So compute:

- A2 (2s-1)/(s+2s^2-1) + (1-4s^2) =? Let's denote D = s+2s^2-1. Then we need to compute expression E = (1-4s^2) - (2s-1)(1-3s+4s^3)/D. Then Oy = -C2 / (c E). C2 = 2s(1-s-2s^2). Note that 1-s-2s^2 = -(2s^2+s-1)? Actually, 2s^2+s-1 = (2s-1)(s+1). So 1-s-2s^2 = -(2s^2+s-1) = -(2s-1)(s+1). So C2 = 2s * (-(2s-1)(s+1)) = -2s(2s-1)(s+1). So -C2 = 2s(2s-1)(s+1). That is positive. So Oy = 2s(2s-1)(s+1) / (c E). Good.

Now, compute E = (1-4s^2) - (2s-1)(1-3s+4s^3)/D. Let's simplify algebraically. Compute D = s+2s^2-1 = (2s^2+s-1). And note that (2s-1)(s+1)=2s^2+s-1 = D. So D = (2s-1)(s+1). That is nice. So we can replace D.

Now, (2s-1)(1-3s+4s^3)/D = (2s-1)(1-3s+4s^3) / ((2s-1)(s+1)) = (1-3s+4s^3)/(s+1). So E = (1-4s^2) - (1-3s+4s^3)/(s+1). Then:

E = (1-4s^2) - (1-3s+4s^3)/(s+1). Put over common denominator (s+1): = [ (1-4s^2)(s+1) - (1-3s+4s^3) ] / (s+1). Compute numerator:

(1-4s^2)(s+1) = (1)(s+1) -4s^2(s+1) = s+1 -4s^3 -4s^2 = s+1 -4s^2 -4s^3. Then subtract (1-3s+4s^3): gives (s+1 -4s^2 -4s^3) -1 +3s -4s^3 = (s+1-1) +3s -4s^2 -4s^3-4s^3 = s+3s -4s^2 -8s^3 = 4s -4s^2 -8s^3 = 4s(1 - s - 2s^2). So numerator = 4s(1-s-2s^2). Then E = [4s(1-s-2s^2)] / (s+1). So E = 4s(1-s-2s^2)/(s+1). Note that 1-s-2s^2 = -(2s^2+s-1) = -D, so E = 4s(-D)/(s+1) = -4s D/(s+1). Then c E = c * (-4s D/(s+1)) = -4s c D/(s+1). So Oy = 2s(2s-1)(s+1) / (c * (-4s D/(s+1))) = 2s(2s-1)(s+1) * (s+1) / (c * (-4s D)) = (2s(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (-4s c D) = (2s(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (-4s c D). Cancel s (nonzero): = (2(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (-4 c D) = - (2(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (4 c D) = - ((2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (2 c D). Now D = (2s-1)(s+1). So (2s-1) cancels (nonzero since s>0.5). Then Oy = - ((s+1)^2) / (2 c (s+1)) = - (s+1) / (2 c). So Oy = - (s+1)/(2 c). Good!

Now, Ox = - (B1/A1) Oy. B1 = c(2s-1), A1 = s+2s^2-1 = D = (2s-1)(s+1). So B1/A1 = c(2s-1) / ((2s-1)(s+1)) = c/(s+1). So Ox = - (c/(s+1)) * Oy = - (c/(s+1)) * ( - (s+1)/(2c) ) = (c/(s+1)) * ((s+1)/(2c)) = 1/2. So Ox = 1/2. Thus O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). That is a nice result: The circumcenter of triangle B P F is at (0.5, - (s+1)/(2c)). So it lies on the vertical line x=0.5, which is the perpendicular bisector of MN (since M=(0,0), N=(1,0)). Indeed, the circumcenter of B, P, F is on the line x=0.5.

Now, the radius R_circ = distance from O to any of B,P,F. Let's compute distance to B. B = (1-2s^2, -2s c). O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). So dx = 1-2s^2 - 1/2 = 1/2 - 2s^2? Actually 1-2s^2 - 1/2 = 1/2 - 2s^2. dy = -2s c - ( - (s+1)/(2c) ) = -2s c + (s+1)/(2c). So distance squared = (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + ( -2s c + (s+1)/(2c) )^2. We can simplify later. But maybe we don't need explicit radius; we need to verify that line L through H parallel to v is tangent to this circle.

Now we have H = (s, - s(1-s)/c). And v = direction of AP = ((2s-1)(s+1), -c(1+2s)). So line L: (x,y) = H + t v.

Now we need to show that L is tangent to circle with center O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). This condition is that the distance from O to L equals the radius R_circ. Alternatively, we can show that the line L is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency, i.e., that O to L is perpendicular to v. Since L is parallel to v, the distance from O to L is |(O-H) × v|/|v|. We need that equals R_circ.

We can compute O-H = (1/2 - s, - (s+1)/(2c) + s(1-s)/c). Compute:

Ox_H = s, Oy_H = - s(1-s)/c.
So O-H = (1/2 - s, - (s+1)/(2c) + s(1-s)/c). Combine y: - (s+1)/(2c) + s(1-s)/c = (1/c)[ - (s+1)/2 + s(1-s) ] = (1/c)[ s(1-s) - (s+1)/2 ]. Compute s(1-s) = s - s^2. So expression = (1/c)[ s - s^2 - (s+1)/2 ] = (1/c)[ (2s - 2s^2 - s -1)/2 ] = (1/c)[ (s - 2s^2 -1)/2 ] = (1/(2c))(s - 2s^2 -1). So O-H = (1/2 - s, (s - 2s^2 -1)/(2c)). Note that 1/2 - s = -(s - 1/2). And s - 2s^2 -1 = -(2s^2 - s +1) = -(2s^2 - s +1). So we can write:

O-H = ( - (s - 1/2), - (2s^2 - s +1)/(2c) ). Actually, s - 2s^2 -1 = -(2s^2 - s +1). So O-H = (1/2 - s, -(2s^2 - s +1)/(2c)). Good.

Now compute cross product (O-H) × v. Since we are in 2D, cross product scalar = (O-H)_x * v_y - (O-H)_y * v_x. Compute v = (v_x, v_y) = ((2s-1)(s+1), -c(1+2s)). Note that (2s-1)(s+1) = D = (2s-1)(s+1). And c = √(1-s^2). We'll keep.

So compute:

(O-H)_x * v_y = (1/2 - s) * ( -c(1+2s) ) = -(1/2 - s) c(1+2s) = (s - 1/2) c(1+2s).
(O-H)_y * v_x = [ -(2s^2 - s +1)/(2c) ] * [ (2s-1)(s+1) ] = -(2s^2 - s +1)/(2c) * D. So (O-H)_y * v_x = -(2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c). Then cross = (O-H)_x v_y - (O-H)_y v_x = (s-1/2)c(1+2s) - [ -(2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c) ] = (s-1/2)c(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c).

Now, note that D = (2s-1)(s+1). And (s-1/2) = (2s-1)/2. So (s-1/2)c(1+2s) = ((2s-1)/2) c (1+2s). So cross = ((2s-1)c(1+2s))/2 + (2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c). Put over common denominator 2c: = (1/(2c))[ (2s-1)c^2(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D ]. Because (2s-1)c(1+2s) * c? Actually, first term: ((2s-1)c(1+2s))/2 = ((2s-1)c(1+2s))/2. Multiply numerator and denominator to combine with second term? Better: cross = 1/(2) [ (2s-1)c(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D/c ]. Write as (1/(2c))[ (2s-1)c^2(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D ].

Now compute the bracket B = (2s-1)c^2(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D. Since c^2 = 1-s^2, we have (2s-1)(1-s^2)(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D. And D = (2s-1)(s+1). So we can factor (2s-1) maybe. Let's compute:

First term: (2s-1)(1-s^2)(1+2s). Second term: (2s^2 - s +1) (2s-1)(s+1) = (2s-1)(s+1)(2s^2 - s +1). So B = (2s-1)[ (1-s^2)(1+2s) + (s+1)(2s^2 - s +1) ].

Now compute the inner sum: (1-s^2)(1+2s) + (s+1)(2s^2 - s +1). Expand:

(1-s^2)(1+2s) = (1)(1+2s) - s^2(1+2s)=1+2s - s^2 -2s^3.
(s+1)(2s^2 - s +1) = (s)(2s^2 - s +1) + 1*(2s^2 - s +1)=2s^3 - s^2 + s +2s^2 - s +1 = 2s^3 + s^2 + 0s +1? Let's do: 2s^3 - s^2 + s +2s^2 - s +1 = 2s^3 + (-s^2+2s^2)=+s^2, and s - s =0, so +1. So sum = 2s^3 + s^2 + 1. Then total inner = (1+2s - s^2 -2s^3) + (2s^3 + s^2 +1) = 1+2s - s^2 -2s^3 +2s^3 + s^2 +1 = 2 + 2s. So inner = 2(1+s). Thus B = (2s-1)*2(1+s)=2(2s-1)(1+s). So cross = (1/(2c)) * B = (1/(2c)) * 2(2s-1)(1+s) = (2s-1)(1+s)/c.

Now, |v| = PA = √(2(1+s)). So distance from O to L = |cross|/|v| = |(2s-1)(1+s)/c| / √(2(1+s)) = (2s-1)(1+s)/(c) * 1/√(2(1+s)) = (2s-1)√(1+s)/(c√2). Because (1+s)/√(1+s)=√(1+s). So distance = (2s-1)√(1+s)/(c√2).

Now compute radius R_circ = distance from O to B. O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). B = (1-2s^2, -2s c). Compute squared distance:

dx = 1-2s^2 - 1/2 = 1/2 - 2s^2.
dy = -2s c + (s+1)/(2c) = (1/(2c))( -4s c^2 + s+1 ) = (1/(2c))( -4s(1-s^2) + s+1 ) = (1/(2c))( -4s +4s^3 + s+1 ) = (1/(2c))( -3s +4s^3 +1 ) = (1/(2c))(1 -3s +4s^3). So R_circ^2 = (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + [ (1 -3s +4s^3)/(2c) ]^2 = (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2/(4c^2). We need to show that this equals the square of the distance from O to L: d^2 = (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2). Because d = (2s-1)√(1+s)/(c√2) => d^2 = (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2).

So we need to verify that:

(1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2/(4c^2) = (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2).

We can compute (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 = (1/4) - 2s^2 + 4s^4? Actually, (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 = 1/4 - 2*(1/2)*(2s^2) + 4s^4 = 1/4 - 2s^2 + 4s^4. So left side = 1/4 - 2s^2 + 4s^4 + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2/(4c^2). Multiply both sides by 4c^2 to clear denominators? Let's do.

Left side * 4c^2 = 4c^2(1/4 - 2s^2 + 4s^4) + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2 = c^2(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4) + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2.

Right side * 4c^2 = 4c^2 * (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2) = 2 (2s-1)^2 (1+s). So we need to show:

c^2(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4) + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2 = 2 (2s-1)^2 (1+s).

Now, c^2 = 1-s^2. So compute c^2(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4) = (1-s^2)(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4). Expand: = (1)(1-8s^2+16s^4) - s^2(1-8s^2+16s^4) = 1 -8s^2+16s^4 - s^2 +8s^4 -16s^6 = 1 -9s^2 +24s^4 -16s^6.

Now compute (1 -3s +4s^3)^2 = 1 +9s^2 +16s^6 -6s +8s^3 -24s^4? Let's do carefully: (a+b+c)^2 with a=1, b=-3s, c=4s^3. Then a^2=1, b^2=9s^2, c^2=16s^6, 2ab=2*1*(-3s)=-6s, 2ac=2*1*4s^3=8s^3, 2bc=2*(-3s)*(4s^3)=-24s^4. So sum = 1 -6s +9s^2 +8s^3 -24s^4 +16s^6. Then total left = (1 -9s^2 +24s^4 -16s^6) + (1 -6s +9s^2 +8s^3 -24s^4 +16s^6) = combine: constants: 1+1=2; s term: -6s; s^2: -9s^2+9s^2=0; s^3: 8s^3; s^4: 24s^4-24s^4=0; s^6: -16s^6+16s^6=0. So left = 2 -6s +8s^3 = 2(1 -3s +4s^3). So left = 2(1-3s+4s^3). Right side: 2(2s-1)^2(1+s) = 2( (2s-1)^2 (1+s) ). Now, (2s-1)^2 = 4s^2 -4s +1. So (2s-1)^2 (1+s) = (4s^2-4s+1)(1+s) = (4s^2-4s+1)*1 + (4s^2-4s+1)*s = 4s^2-4s+1 +4s^3-4s^2+s = 4s^2-4s+1+4s^3-4s^2+s = 4s^3 -3s +1. So right = 2(4s^3 -3s +1) = 2(1 -3s +4s^3). That matches left. So identity holds. Thus distance from O to L equals radius. Therefore L is tangent to circumcircle of triangle B P F.

Thus we have proven the statement under the assumption that r=1 (scale). But we also scaled such that M=0 and N=1? Actually we set M=(0,0), N=(1,0) and r=1. This is a valid coordinate choice because we can scale and translate the whole configuration. The condition that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of BEF is invariant under similarity transformations (translation, rotation, scaling). So proving it for this specific coordinate system suffices for the general case. However, we must ensure that we didn't lose generality by setting r=1 and MN=1. We have M=(0,0), N=(1,0). Let r and R be radii with r<R and R-r < 1 < R+r (intersection). Then:

Now, P = circumcenter of triangle A, C, D. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (C+D)/2 = (-r + 1+R)/2 = (1+R-r)/2 = X. So P = (X, y_P). And y_P is determined by PA = PC (or PA = PD). Compute PC^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2. PA^2 = (X - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2. Set equal:

(X - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2. Expand:

X^2 -2X x0 + x0^2 + y_P^2 -2y_P y0 + y0^2 = X^2 +2X r + r^2 + y_P^2. Cancel X^2,y_P^2:

-2X x0 + x0^2 -2y_P y0 + y0^2 = 2X r + r^2.

Now, x0^2+y0^2 = r^2. So x0^2+y0^2 - r^2 =0. So we have:

-2X x0 -2y_P y0 = 2X r + r^2 - r^2 = 2X r. Wait: Bring terms: -2X x0 + x0^2 -2y_P y0 + y0^2 = 2X r + r^2. Since x0^2+y0^2 = r^2, then left = -2X x0 -2y_P y0 + r^2. So equation: -2X x0 -2y_P y0 + r^2 = 2X r + r^2 => -2X x0 -2y_P y0 = 2X r => Divide by -2: X x0 + y_P y0 = -X r. So y_P y0 = -X(x0+r). Thus y_P = -X(x0+r)/y0. That is our earlier expression.

Now, we also have from geometry: X = (1+R-r)/2. And x0 = (1+r^2-R^2)/2. Then x0+r = (1+r^2-R^2 + 2r)/2 = (1 + 2r + r^2 - R^2)/2 = ((1+r)^2 - R^2)/2 = ((1+r-R)(1+r+R))/2. So y_P = -X * ((1+r-R)(1+r+R)/2) / y0. And y0 = √(r^2-x0^2). We can also express y0 in terms of r,R.

Given the algebra is getting heavy, but perhaps we can find a simpler parameterization. Note that we have a free parameter r and R. We have that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). In our coordinate proof for the special case, we found that the distance from O to L equals radius. That came from a simplification where O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)) and L had direction v. In the general case, we might expect that O = (1/2, something) and that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent. Let's try to see if we can find O in the general case using the same method as before. We can set up equations for O = (Ox, Oy) such that distances to B, P, F are equal. That will give two linear equations (since the constant terms cancel if we subtract appropriately). Let's attempt that.

We have:

B = (x0, -y0)
P = (X, y_P)
F = (F_x, F_y)

We want:

|O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2   (1)
|O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2   (2)

From (1): (Ox - x0)^2 + (Oy + y0)^2 = (Ox - X)^2 + (Oy - y_P)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2Ox x0 + x0^2 + Oy^2 +2Oy y0 + y0^2 = Ox^2 -2Ox X + X^2 + Oy^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2.

Cancel Ox^2, Oy^2:

-2Ox x0 + x0^2 +2Oy y0 + y0^2 = -2Ox X + X^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2.

Bring terms:

-2Ox x0 +2Ox X +2Oy y0 +2Oy y_P + x0^2 + y0^2 - X^2 - y_P^2 =0 => 2Ox (X - x0) + 2Oy (y0 + y_P) + (x0^2+y0^2 - X^2 - y_P^2)=0.

Now, x0^2+y0^2 = r^2. And X^2+y_P^2 =? X^2+y_P^2 = ( (1+s)/2 )^2 + ( (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) )^2 = (1+s)^2/4 + (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(4(t^2-1)) = (1/4)[ (1+s)^2 + (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

But note that (1+s)^2 = 1+2s+s^2. And (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(t^2-1) = (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(t^2-1). Not obviously simplifying to something like? However, we might compute the constant term K = r^2 - X^2 - y_P^2. That is not zero generally. So equation (1) becomes:

2Ox (X-x0) + 2Oy (y0 + y_P) + K = 0. (1)

Now from (2): (Ox - X)^2 + (Oy - y_P)^2 = (Ox - F_x)^2 + (Oy - F_y)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2Ox X + X^2 + Oy^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2 = Ox^2 -2Ox F_x + F_x^2 + Oy^2 -2Oy F_y + F_y^2.

Cancel Ox^2,Oy^2:

-2Ox X + X^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2 = -2Ox F_x + F_x^2 -2Oy F_y + F_y^2.

Bring terms:

-2Ox X +2Ox F_x -2Oy y_P +2Oy F_y + X^2 + y_P^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2 =0 => 2Ox (F_x - X) + 2Oy (F_y - y_P) + (X^2+y_P^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2)=0. (2)

Thus we have two linear equations in Ox, Oy. If we can show that these equations are consistent and that the solution (Ox, Oy) lies on the vertical line x=1/2, and that the distance from O to line L equals the radius, then we are done. But the algebra is heavy.

Given the difficulty, maybe we can find a synthetic proof. Let's think about the configuration more.

We have two circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Let M,N be centers. Let C and D be the points on MN such that C is on Ω and D on Γ, with order C-M-N-D. So we have that M is the midpoint of the arc? Not.

Define P as circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the point such that PA=PC=PD. This means that P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. So P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Note that A and C are on Ω, and A and D on Γ. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. This circle is sometimes called the circumcircle of the triangle formed by one intersection and the two far points.

Now, consider the line through P parallel to MN? Not.

Another observation: Since MA=MC, M lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, N lies on the perpendicular bisector of AD. So P is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. So P is the point such that PM ⟂ AC and PN ⟂ AD. So ∠(PM, AC)=90° and ∠(PN, AD)=90°. Also, note that AC is a chord of Ω, and AD is a chord of Γ.

Now, consider the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since M and N lie on the line MN, the altitude from P is the line through P perpendicular to MN. So H lies on that line. Also, the altitude from M is the line through M perpendicular to PN. So H is intersection of these altitudes.

Now, we want to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since E is on Ω and F on Γ, and A, E, P, F are collinear. So AP is a line through A and P. The line through H parallel to AP is L. So we need to show that L is tangent to circle (BEF). Perhaps we can show that L is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF)? Or that A lies on the radical axis of some circles.

Alternatively, maybe we can use the concept of the Miquel point. Consider the quadrilateral formed by the two circles and the line MN? Not.

Let's try to find a relation between H and the points B, E, F. In our coordinate proof for the special case, we found that O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)) and that the distance from O to L equals radius. Let's try to set up coordinates with M=(0,0), N=(d,0), and let the circles have equations: Ω: x^2+y^2 = r^2, Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 = R^2. We have r<R and circles intersect. We can also define the points using parameters that reflect the intersection. For example, we can set A = (r cosθ, r sinθ) for some θ. Then the condition that A lies on Γ gives (r cosθ - d)^2 + (r sinθ)^2 = R^2 => r^2 + d^2 -2dr cosθ = R^2. So R^2 = r^2 + d^2 -2dr cosθ. That's fine.

Then C = (-r, 0) because on Ω and on line MN (x-axis). D = (d+R, 0) because on Γ and on line MN. So X = (C+D)/2 = (d+R-r)/2. Then P = (X, y_P) with y_P from PA=PC. We can compute y_P as before: y_P = -X (1+cosθ? Actually, careful: In this normalization, A is on Ω, so its coordinates are (r cosθ, r sinθ). Then we have x0 = r cosθ, y0 = r sinθ. Then we have X = (d+R-r)/2. Then the condition PA=PC gives: (X - r cosθ)^2 + (y_P - r sinθ)^2 = (X+r)^2 + y_P^2. Simplify to get: y_P = -X (1 + (r cosθ)/r? Let's derive: From earlier general formula with M=(0,0), N=(d,0), and Ω radius r, we had: y_P = -X (x0+r)/y0, where x0 = r cosθ, y0 = r sinθ. So y_P = -X (r cosθ + r)/(r sinθ) = -X (1+ cosθ)/ sinθ = -X cot(θ/2). So P = (X, -X cot(θ/2)). That's simple.

Now, we need F, the second intersection of line AP with Γ. Compute v = P-A = (X - r cosθ, -X cot(θ/2) - r sinθ). We can simplify v using half-angle: r sinθ = 2r sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2), cot(θ/2)=cos(θ/2)/sin(θ/2). So v = (X - r cosθ, -X cos(θ/2)/sin(θ/2) - 2r sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) = -cos(θ/2)[ X/sin(θ/2) + 2r sin(θ/2) ] = -cos(θ/2)[ (X + 2r sin^2(θ/2))/ sin(θ/2) ].

Now, we need u_F = -2 (A-N)·v / |v|^2. A-N = (r cosθ - d, r sinθ). Compute dot product (A-N)·v. This will be messy, but maybe we can find that u_F = (d+R)/? Not.

Given the time, perhaps we can accept that the proof for the special case is not general, but maybe we can argue that the problem is symmetric and we can always apply a similarity transformation that sends the circle Ω to the unit circle and also sends the point N to (1,0) by scaling? That would set d=1 and r=1, but we cannot do that simultaneously unless we have a specific ratio. However, note that the problem statement does not fix the distance between centers; it is given. So we can choose coordinates such that M=(0,0) and N=(1,0) by scaling. That is always possible. So we have a general configuration with MN=1. Now, we need to prove the statement for all r. Our earlier coordinate derivation for the general case with MN=1 gave us expressions in terms of s = R-r, t = R+r. We also derived that (A-N)·w = -(s+t)/2, which gave a nice expression for u_F = (t-1)/r? But that was for the case r=1? Wait, we derived that (A-N)·w = -(s+t)/2, and then u_F = -4/(1+t) * ( -(s+t)/2 ) / |w|^2 = (2(s+t)/(1+t)) / |w|^2. And then we used that |w|^2 = (t^2-s^2)/(t^2-1) = (4rR)/(t^2-1) maybe. But we then got u_F = (t-1)/r after simplification? That derivation assumed r=1? Let's check: We had u_F = -4/(1+t) * (A-N)·w / |w|^2, and we computed (A-N)·w = -(s+t)/2. So u_F = -4/(1+t) * (-(s+t)/2) / |w|^2 = (2(s+t)/(1+t)) / |w|^2. Now, we need to compute |w|^2. We had w = ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) )? Actually, we defined w = (s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1)). That was from earlier: w = ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ). But that expression came from the definition of w = (v_x, v_y) factor? Let's re-derive: We had v = ((1+t)/2) w, where w = ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ). And we computed (A-N)·w = -(s+t)/2. Then u_F = (2(s+t)/(1+t)) / |w|^2. Now, |w|^2 = s^2 + t^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) = (s^2 (t^2-1) + t^2(1-s^2))/(t^2-1) = (s^2 t^2 - s^2 + t^2 - t^2 s^2)/(t^2-1) = (t^2 - s^2)/(t^2-1) = (t^2-s^2)/(t^2-1). And t^2-s^2 = (R+r)^2 - (R-r)^2 = 4rR. So |w|^2 = 4rR/(t^2-1). Then u_F = (2(s+t)/(1+t)) * (t^2-1)/(4rR). Now, s+t = 2R. So u_F = (2*2R/(1+t)) * (t^2-1)/(4rR) = (4R/(1+t)) * (t^2-1)/(4rR) = (t^2-1)/(r(1+t)) = (t-1)(t+1)/(r(1+t)) = (t-1)/r. So u_F = (t-1)/r. That is a general expression, valid for any r (since we used the definitions of s and t in terms of r and R, and the derivation used only that v = ((1+t)/2) w, which came from the expressions for v_x and v_y in terms of s and t. Let's verify: v_x = s(1+t)/2. v_y = - (√(1-s^2) t(1+t))/(2√(t^2-1)). So indeed v = ((1+t)/2) * ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ) = ((1+t)/2) w, where w = ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ). That expression is valid for any r, because we derived v_x = s(1+t)/2, v_y = - (√(1-s^2) t(1+t))/(2√(t^2-1)). And we also have that r = (t-s)/2, R = (t+s)/2. So u_F = (t-1)/r. That is a nice simplification.

Thus F = A + u_F v = A + ((t-1)/r) * v. Since v = ((1+t)/2) w, we have F = A + ((t-1)(1+t))/(2r) w = A + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) w. And w = ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ). So F = A + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ) = A + ( (t^2-1)s/(2r), - t √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1)/(2r) ). But note that (t^2-1)/(2r) = (t-1)(t+1)/(2r). Not sure.

Now, we have B = (x0, -y0) = ((1-st)/2, - (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2))). And we have P = (X, y_P) = ((1+s)/2, - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1))). And F we can compute.

Now, we want to compute the circumcenter O of triangle B, P, F. This will give two linear equations in Ox, Oy. Perhaps we can find that O lies on the line x=1/2? Let's test with our numeric example where MN=1, r=0.5, R=1.3, s=0.8, t=1.8. We computed O ≈ (0.0947, -2.953). That is not x=0.5. So O is not on that line. So the circumcenter is not simply located.

But maybe we can find that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF) by computing the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and showing that the distance from H to the circle's center is something? Actually, for a line L to be tangent to a circle, the condition is that the power of any point on L with respect to the circle equals the square of the distance from that point to the point of tangency? Not directly.

We can compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then compute the distance from H to the line L, and also compute the distance from H to the center O, and show that the distance from O to L equals the radius. That is what we did in the special case. In the general case, we can attempt to do that algebraically. Since we have expressions in terms of s and t, we can try to compute O and L and verify the tangency condition. This is heavy but maybe we can find that the condition simplifies to an identity. Let's attempt to do that with the help of some systematic algebra.

We have points:

B = ( (1 - s t)/2, - (1/2) √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ).
P = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) ).
F = A + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) w, where A = ( (1-st)/2, (1/2) √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) )? Actually, y0 = (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). So A = ( (1-st)/2, (1/2) √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ). And w = ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ). So:

F = ( (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s/(2r), (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) * ( - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = ( (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s/(2r), (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) - ((t^2-1)/(2r)) * t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ).

Simplify the y-coordinate: (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) = (1/2)√(t^2-1)√(1-s^2). And the second term: ((t^2-1)/(2r)) * t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = (t √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1))/(2r). So F_y = (1/2)√(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) - (t √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1))/(2r) = √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) ( 1/2 - t/(2r) ) = √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) * ( (r - t)/(2r) ). Since r = (t-s)/2, then r - t = (t-s)/2 - t = (t-s-2t)/2 = (-t-s)/2 = -(t+s)/2. So (r-t)/(2r) = -(t+s)/(4r). Thus F_y = - √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) * (t+s)/(4r). But t+s = 2R, so F_y = - (2R)/(4r) √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) = - (R/(2r)) √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2). And note that √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) = √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). So F_y = - (R/(2r)) √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). That is a nice expression.

Now, F_x = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(2r). Write r = (t-s)/2, so 2r = t-s. So ((t^2-1)s)/(2r) = ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). So F_x = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). Combine: = [ (1-st)(t-s) + 2s(t^2-1) ] / [2(t-s)]. Expand numerator: (1-st)(t-s) = (t-s) - st(t-s) = t-s - s t^2 + s^2 t. Then add 2s(t^2-1) = 2s t^2 - 2s. Sum = t-s - s t^2 + s^2 t + 2s t^2 - 2s = t-s + s t^2 + s^2 t - 2s = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - s - 2s? Actually, -s - 2s = -3s. So numerator = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s. So F_x = (t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s) / [2(t-s)]. That's messy. But maybe we can also express F_x in terms of A and something. Note that A_x = (1-st)/2, so F_x = A_x + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). That is fine.

Now, we also have P_x = (1+s)/2, P_y = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). And B = ( (1-st)/2, - (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ). So B_x = A_x, B_y = - A_y.

Now, we need H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. With M=(0,0), N=(1,0), P as above. We already derived y_H = - s (1-s)/c? Wait, that was for r=1 case. In general, we need to compute H. Using the same method as before: altitude from P is vertical line x = P_x = (1+s)/2. Altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. PN: from P to N = (1 - P_x, -P_y) = (1 - (1+s)/2, -P_y) = ((2 - (1+s))/2, -P_y) = ((1-s)/2, -P_y). So slope of PN = (-P_y)/((1-s)/2) = -2P_y/(1-s). Perpendicular slope = (1-s)/(2P_y) (since negative reciprocal). So line through M with that slope: y = ((1-s)/(2P_y)) x. Intersection with x = P_x gives y_H = ((1-s)/(2P_y)) * P_x = ((1-s)/(2P_y)) * ((1+s)/2) = (1-s^2)/(4P_y). Now, P_y is negative, so y_H is negative? Actually, 1-s^2>0, P_y negative, so y_H negative. So H = (P_x, y_H) = ((1+s)/2, (1-s^2)/(4P_y)). Now, we need to compute P_y: P_y = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). So 4P_y = - 2(1+t)√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1). Then y_H = (1-s^2) / ( - 2(1+t)√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = - (1-s^2) √(t^2-1) / (2(1+t)√(1-s^2)) = - √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1) / (2(1+t)) because (1-s^2)/√(1-s^2)=√(1-s^2). So y_H = - √((1-s^2)(t^2-1)) / (2(1+t)). Note that √((1-s^2)(t^2-1)) = √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). And 1+t is positive. So H = ( (1+s)/2, - √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) / (2(1+t)) ). That is a nice expression.

Now, the line L through H parallel to AP. Direction v = P-A. We have A = ( (1-st)/2, (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ). P = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) ). So v = ( (1+s)/2 - (1-st)/2, - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) - (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ) = ( (1+s -1 + st)/2, - (1/2)[ (1+t)√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) + √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) ] ) = ( (s + st)/2, - (√(1-s^2)/2)[ (1+t)/√(t^2-1) + √(t^2-1) ] ) = ( s(1+t)/2, - (√(1-s^2)/2)[ (1+t + (t^2-1))/√(t^2-1) ] ) = ( s(1+t)/2, - (√(1-s^2)/2)[ (t^2+t)/√(t^2-1) ] ) = ( s(1+t)/2, - (√(1-s^2) t(1+t))/(2√(t^2-1)) ). So v = (1+t)/2 * ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = (1+t)/2 w, where w = ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ). So v is proportional to w.

Thus line L: (x,y) = H + λ w, λ real.

Now, we need to show that L is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. That is, the distance from the center O of circle (BEF) to L equals the radius. We can try to compute O using the linear equations we derived earlier, but now with the general expressions. That might be doable.

Given the time, I think we should attempt to compute O using the fact that we have expressions for B, P, F in terms of s,t. We'll use the notation:

Let’s define:

U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). Note that U = √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)).

Then we have:

B = ( (1-st)/2, -U/2 )
P = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) ) = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) )
F = ( (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s), - (R/(2r)) U ) with R = (t+s)/2, r = (t-s)/2, so R/(2r) = (t+s)/(2*(t-s))? Actually, R/(2r) = ((t+s)/2) / (2*(t-s)/2) = (t+s)/(2(t-s)). So F_y = - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) * U.

Now, note that B_y = -U/2, so F_y is something like -U*(t+s)/(2(t-s)). And P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). But we can also write P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) = - (1+t) / (2) * √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = - (1+t)/(2) * (√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1)). Not directly U.

Now, we need to compute O. We can set up the equations:

From |O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2:
(O_x - B_x)^2 + (O_y - B_y)^2 = (O_x - P_x)^2 + (O_y - P_y)^2.
Expand: (O_x^2 -2O_x B_x + B_x^2) + (O_y^2 -2O_y B_y + B_y^2) = (O_x^2 -2O_x P_x + P_x^2) + (O_y^2 -2O_y P_y + P_y^2). Cancel O_x^2,O_y^2:
-2O_x B_x -2O_y B_y + B_x^2+B_y^2 = -2O_x P_x -2O_y P_y + P_x^2+P_y^2.
Bring terms:
2O_x (P_x - B_x) + 2O_y (P_y - B_y) + (B_x^2+B_y^2 - P_x^2 - P_y^2) = 0. (Eq1)

Similarly, from |O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2:
2O_x (F_x - P_x) + 2O_y (F_y - P_y) + (P_x^2+P_y^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2) = 0. (Eq2)

We can compute the constants.

First, compute B_x = (1-st)/2, P_x = (1+s)/2, so P_x - B_x = (1+s - (1-st))/2 = (s + st)/2 = s(1+t)/2 = s(1+t)/2.

Now, B_y = -U/2, P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). Let's compute P_y - B_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) + U/2 = (1/2)[ U - (1+t) √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ].
But U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). So U - (1+t) √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = √(1-s^2)[ √(t^2-1) - (1+t)/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t^2-1 - (1+t))/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t^2-1 -1 - t)/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t^2 - t -2)/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t-2)(t+1)/√(t^2-1) ]? Because t^2 - t -2 = (t-2)(t+1). Yes. So P_y - B_y = (1/2) √(1-s^2) * (t-2)(t+1)/√(t^2-1) = (1/2) √(1-s^2) (t-2)(t+1)/√(t^2-1). That is not obviously simplifying.

Now, compute the constant term K1 = B_x^2+B_y^2 - P_x^2 - P_y^2. B_x^2+B_y^2 = ( (1-st)^2/4 + U^2/4 ) = ( (1-st)^2 + U^2 )/4. P_x^2+P_y^2 = ( (1+s)^2/4 + (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(4(t^2-1)) ) = ( (1+s)^2 + (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) )/4. So K1 = (1/4)[ (1-st)^2 + U^2 - (1+s)^2 - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

Now, U^2 = (t^2-1)(1-s^2). So (1-st)^2 + U^2 = (1 - 2st + s^2 t^2) + (t^2-1)(1-s^2) = 1 - 2st + s^2 t^2 + (t^2-1) - (t^2-1)s^2 = 1 - 2st + s^2 t^2 + t^2 -1 - s^2 t^2 + s^2 = (1-1) + t^2 - 2st + s^2 = t^2 - 2st + s^2 = (t-s)^2. So (1-st)^2+U^2 = (t-s)^2. Then K1 = (1/4)[ (t-s)^2 - (1+s)^2 - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

Now, (t-s)^2 - (1+s)^2 = (t^2 -2st + s^2) - (1+2s+s^2) = t^2 -2st + s^2 -1 -2s - s^2 = t^2 -2st -1 -2s = (t^2 -1) - 2s(t+1). So K1 = (1/4)[ (t^2-1) - 2s(t+1) - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

Now, (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) = (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1). So K1 = (1/4)[ (t^2-1) - 2s(t+1) - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

This is not zero generally. So Eq1 becomes:

2O_x * (s(1+t)/2) + 2O_y * (P_y - B_y) + K1 = 0 => O_x s(1+t) + 2O_y (P_y - B_y) + K1 = 0. (Eq1')

Now, compute Eq2: 2O_x (F_x - P_x) + 2O_y (F_y - P_y) + (P_x^2+P_y^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2) = 0.

We need F_x - P_x and F_y - P_y. Compute F_x = A_x + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). A_x = (1-st)/2. So F_x = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). P_x = (1+s)/2. So F_x - P_x = (1-st)/2 - (1+s)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s) = ( -2s)/2? Actually, (1-st -1 - s)/2 = (-s - st)/2 = -s(1+t)/2. So F_x - P_x = -s(1+t)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s) = s[ - (1+t)/2 + (t^2-1)/(t-s) ].

Simplify the bracket: - (1+t)/2 + (t^2-1)/(t-s) = - (1+t)/2 + (t-1)(t+1)/(t-s). Write (t^2-1)/(t-s) = (t-1)(t+1)/(t-s). So bracket = (t+1)[ -1/2 + (t-1)/(t-s) ] = (t+1)[ ( - (t-s) + 2(t-1) ) / (2(t-s)) ] = (t+1)[ (-t+s+2t-2) / (2(t-s)) ] = (t+1)[ (t + s -2) / (2(t-s)) ].

Thus F_x - P_x = s (t+1) (t+s-2) / (2(t-s)). So that is one.

Now, F_y - P_y. We have F_y = - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) * U. P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). But U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). So F_y = - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) * √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). So a_x^2 - c^2 = -a_y^2. Then plug: 2a_y y_P = 2(-(r+a_x))X + (-a_y^2) + a_y^2 = -2(r+a_x)X. So 2a_y y_P = -2(r+a_x)X => y_P = -(r+a_x)X / a_y.

Now X = (d+R-r)/2. So y_P = -(r+a_x) * (d+R-r)/(2 a_y). Since a_x = x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So r+a_x = r + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = (2dr + d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = (d^2 + 2dr + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)/(2d) = ((d+r - R)(d+r+R))/(2d). But maybe we can simplify later.

Also a_y = y0 = sqrt(r^2 - a_x^2). Not needed now.

Let's attempt to compute coordinates.

Set d = MN = 1. Then:

x0 = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2.
y0 = sqrt(r^2 - x0^2). Positive.

C = (-r, 0).
D = (1+R, 0).

X = (1+R - r)/2.
y_P = - ((1+R)^2 - r^2) / (2 sqrt((R+r)^2 - 1)). Let's denote:

Let’s compute y_P more simply: y_P = - ( (1+R)^2 - r^2 ) / (2 √((R+r)^2-1) ). And note that √((R+r)^2-1) = √((R+r-1)(R+r+1)). We'll keep it.

Now v = P - A = (X - x0, y_P - y0). Compute X - x0:

X - x0 = (1+R-r)/2 - (1+r^2-R^2)/2 = (1+R-r -1 - r^2 + R^2)/2 = (R - r - r^2 + R^2)/2 = (R^2 + R - r^2 - r)/2 = ((R^2 - r^2) + (R - r))/2 = ((R-r)(R+r) + (R-r))/2 = (R-r)(R+r+1)/2.

So v_x = (R-r)(R+r+1)/2. That's positive since R>r. Good.

Now v_y = y_P - y0. y_P negative, y0 positive, so v_y negative. Compute y0 in terms of r,R: y0 = sqrt(r^2 - x0^2). We have x0 = (1+r^2-R^2)/2. So y0 = sqrt( r^2 - ((1+r^2-R^2)/2)^2 ) = (1/2) sqrt(4r^2 - (1+r^2-R^2)^2). As earlier, we expressed a_y = y0 = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(1+R+r)? Wait we had a_y = y0 = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(1+R+r))? Let's recompute: earlier we derived a_y = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(1+R+r)). But careful: we had a_y = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(1+R+r)). That is correct? Let's verify: we had a_y = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(R+r+1)). So yes, y0 = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(R+r+1)). But note that sqrt((R+r-1)(R+r+1)) = sqrt((R+r)^2-1). So y0 = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r)^2-1). So that's a nice expression. Check: earlier we derived a_y = (1+R-r)/2 * sqrt((R+r-1)(1+R+r))? Actually (R+r-1)(R+r+1) = (R+r)^2-1. So yes.

Thus y0 = (1+R-r)/2 * √((R+r)^2-1). And y_P = - ( (1+R)^2 - r^2 ) / (2 √((R+r)^2-1)). So we have:

y0 = (1+R-r)/2 * S, where S = √((R+r)^2-1).
y_P = - ((1+R)^2 - r^2) / (2 S). Note that ((1+R)^2 - r^2) = (1+R-r)(1+R+r) = (1+R-r)(1+R+r). So y_P = - (1+R-r)(1+R+r) / (2 S).

Thus v_y = y_P - y0 = - (1+R-r)(1+R+r)/(2S) - (1+R-r)/2 * S = -(1+R-r)/2 * ( (1+R+r)/S + S ). So v_y = -(1+R-r)/2 * ( (1+R+r + S^2)/S ). But S^2 = (R+r)^2 - 1. So (1+R+r) + S^2 = (1+R+r) + (R+r)^2 -1 = (R+r) + (R+r)^2 = (R+r)(1+R+r). So v_y = -(1+R-r)/2 * ( (R+r)(1+R+r)/S ) = -(1+R-r)(R+r)(1+R+r) / (2 S). That is v_y = - (1+R-r)(R+r)(1+R+r) / (2 S). But we can also write v_y = - (1+R-r)(R+r)(1+R+r) / (2 √((R+r)^2-1)). That's fine.

Now we have v = (v_x, v_y) with v_x = (R-r)(R+r+1)/2. v_y is negative. Also note that v_x >0, v_y <0.

We can simplify v using trig identities.

Actually, we need to be careful with orientation: The line AP: we have points A, P, F. The vector from P to A is A-P. The distance PA = |A-P|. Now, compute PA^2 = |A-P|^2. A-P = (cosθ - sin(θ/2), sinθ + cos(θ/2)). Compute cosθ = 1-2 sin^2(θ/2). So cosθ - sin(θ/2) = 1-2 sin^2(θ/2) - sin(θ/2) = 1 - sin(θ/2) - 2 sin^2(θ/2). Alternatively, we can use the fact that A and P are on unit circle, so chord length AP = 2 sin(∠AOP/2)? Actually, central angle between A and P: A = e^{iθ}, P = e^{i(θ/2+π/2)? Alternatively, we can compute the circle equation: |z - O|^2 = R^2. We need O such that distances to B,P,F equal. We can find O as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Since we have coordinates, we can set up linear equations.

Let O = (Ox, Oy). Then:

|O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2.

From |O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2: (Ox - (1-2s^2))^2 + (Oy - (-2s c))^2 = (Ox - s)^2 + (Oy - (-c))^2. Simplify: (Ox - (1-2s^2))^2 + (Oy + 2s c)^2 = (Ox - s)^2 + (Oy + c)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2Ox(1-2s^2) + (1-2s^2)^2 + Oy^2 +4s c Oy +4s^2 c^2 = Ox^2 -2s Ox + s^2 + Oy^2 +2c Oy + c^2. Cancel Ox^2,Oy^2:

-2Ox(1-2s^2) + (1-2s^2)^2 +4s c Oy +4s^2 c^2 = -2s Ox + s^2 +2c Oy + c^2.

Bring terms:

-2Ox(1-2s^2) +2s Ox +4s c Oy -2c Oy + (1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2 =0.

Compute coefficient for Ox: -2(1-2s^2)+2s = -2+4s^2+2s = 2s+4s^2-2 = 2(s+2s^2-1). Coefficient for Oy: 4s c -2c = 2c(2s-1). So:

2(s+2s^2-1) Ox + 2c(2s-1) Oy + [(1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2] =0. Divide by 2: (s+2s^2-1) Ox + c(2s-1) Oy + 1/2[(1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2] =0.

Now compute the constant term K = (1-2s^2)^2 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2. Expand (1-2s^2)^2 = 1 -4s^2+4s^4. So K = 1 -4s^2+4s^4 - s^2 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2 = 1 -5s^2+4s^4 - c^2 +4s^2 c^2. But c^2 = 1-s^2. So -c^2 = -(1-s^2)= -1+s^2. Then K = 1 -5s^2+4s^4 -1+s^2 +4s^2(1-s^2) = (1-1) + (-5s^2+s^2)= -4s^2 +4s^4 +4s^2(1-s^2)= -4s^2+4s^4+4s^2-4s^4=0. So K=0! So the constant term is zero. So equation reduces to: (s+2s^2-1) Ox + c(2s-1) Oy = 0. (Equation (1))

Now from |O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2: (Ox - s)^2 + (Oy + c)^2 = (Ox - (1-2s+4s^3))^2 + (Oy + 4s^2 c)^2. Expand:

-2s Ox + s^2 +2c Oy + c^2 = -2(1-2s+4s^3) Ox + (1-2s+4s^3)^2 + 8s^2 c Oy + 16s^4 c^2.

Bring terms:

-2s Ox +2(1-2s+4s^3) Ox +2c Oy -8s^2 c Oy + s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2 =0.

Compute coefficient for Ox: -2s +2(1-2s+4s^3) = -2s +2 -4s+8s^3 = 2 -6s +8s^3 = 2(1 -3s +4s^3). Coefficient for Oy: 2c -8s^2 c = 2c(1-4s^2). So equation:

2(1-3s+4s^3) Ox + 2c(1-4s^2) Oy + (s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2) =0. Divide by 2: (1-3s+4s^3) Ox + c(1-4s^2) Oy + 1/2(s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2)=0.

Now compute constant term L = s^2 + c^2 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2. Since c^2=1-s^2, then s^2 + c^2 = s^2 + 1 - s^2 =1. So L = 1 - (1-2s+4s^3)^2 -16s^4 c^2. Compute (1-2s+4s^3)^2. Let’s denote T = 1-2s+4s^3. Compute T^2 = (1)^2 + (-2s)^2 + (4s^3)^2 + 2*1*(-2s) + 2*1*(4s^3) + 2*(-2s)*(4s^3) = 1 +4s^2+16s^6 -4s +8s^3 -16s^4? Wait: 2*(1)*(-2s) = -4s; 2*(1)*(4s^3)=8s^3; 2*(-2s)*(4s^3)= -16s^4. So T^2 = 1 -4s +4s^2 +8s^3 -16s^4 +16s^6. (1-3s+4s^3) Ox + c(1-4s^2) Oy + 2s(1-s-2s^2) =0. (Equation (2))

Now, compute E = (1-4s^2) - (2s-1)(1-3s+4s^3)/D. Let's simplify algebraically. Compute D = s+2s^2-1 = (2s^2+s-1). And note that (2s-1)(s+1)=2s^2+s-1 = D. So D = (2s-1)(s+1). That is nice. So we can replace D.

E = (1-4s^2) - (1-3s+4s^3)/(s+1). Put over common denominator (s+1): = [ (1-4s^2)(s+1) - (1-3s+4s^3) ] / (s+1). Compute numerator:

(1-4s^2)(s+1) = (1)(s+1) -4s^2(s+1) = s+1 -4s^3 -4s^2 = s+1 -4s^2 -4s^3. Then subtract (1-3s+4s^3): gives (s+1 -4s^2 -4s^3) -1 +3s -4s^3 = (s+1-1) +3s -4s^2 -4s^3-4s^3 = s+3s -4s^2 -8s^3 = 4s -4s^2 -8s^3 = 4s(1 - s - 2s^2). So numerator = 4s(1-s-2s^2). Then E = [4s(1-s-2s^2)] / (s+1). So E = 4s(1-s-2s^2)/(s+1). Note that 1-s-2s^2 = -(2s^2+s-1) = -D, so E = 4s(-D)/(s+1) = -4s D/(s+1). Then c E = c * (-4s D/(s+1)) = -4s c D/(s+1). So Oy = 2s(2s-1)(s+1) / (c * (-4s D/(s+1))) = 2s(2s-1)(s+1) * (s+1) / (c * (-4s D)) = (2s(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (-4s c D) = (2s(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (-4s c D). Cancel s (nonzero): = (2(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (-4 c D) = - (2(2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (4 c D) = - ((2s-1)(s+1)^2) / (2 c D). Now D = (2s-1)(s+1). So (2s-1) cancels (nonzero since s>0.5). Then Oy = - ((s+1)^2) / (2 c (s+1)) = - (s+1) / (2 c). So Oy = - (s+1)/(2 c). Good!

Now, Ox = - (B1/A1) Oy. B1 = c(2s-1), A1 = s+2s^2-1 = D = (2s-1)(s+1). So B1/A1 = c(2s-1) / ((2s-1)(s+1)) = c/(s+1). So Ox = - (c/(s+1)) * Oy = - (c/(s+1)) * ( - (s+1)/(2c) ) = (c/(s+1)) * ((s+1)/(2c)) = 1/2. So Ox = 1/2. Thus O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). That is a nice result: The circumcenter of triangle B P F is at (0.5, - (s+1)/(2c)). So it lies on the vertical line x=0.5, which is the perpendicular bisector of MN (since M=(0,0), N=(1,0)). Indeed, the circumcenter of B, P, F is on the line x=0.5.

Now, the radius R_circ = distance from O to any of B,P,F. Let's compute distance to B. B = (1-2s^2, -2s c). O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). So dx = 1-2s^2 - 1/2 = 1/2 - 2s^2? Actually 1-2s^2 - 1/2 = 1/2 - 2s^2. dy = -2s c - ( - (s+1)/(2c) ) = -2s c + (s+1)/(2c). So distance squared = (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + ( -2s c + (s+1)/(2c) )^2. We can simplify later. But maybe we don't need explicit radius; we need to verify that line L through H parallel to v is tangent to this circle.

Now we have H = (s, - s(1-s)/c). And v = direction of AP = ((2s-1)(s+1), -c(1+2s)). So line L: (x,y) = H + t v.

Now we need to show that L is tangent to circle with center O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). This condition is that the distance from O to L equals the radius R_circ. Alternatively, we can show that the line L is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency, i.e., that O to L is perpendicular to v. Since L is parallel to v, the distance from O to L is |(O-H) × v|/|v|. We need that equals R_circ.

We can compute O-H = (1/2 - s, - (s+1)/(2c) + s(1-s)/c). Compute:

Ox_H = s, Oy_H = - s(1-s)/c.
So O-H = (1/2 - s, - (s+1)/(2c) + s(1-s)/c). Combine y: - (s+1)/(2c) + s(1-s)/c = (1/c)[ - (s+1)/2 + s(1-s) ] = (1/c)[ s(1-s) - (s+1)/2 ]. Compute s(1-s) = s - s^2. So expression = (1/c)[ s - s^2 - (s+1)/2 ] = (1/c)[ (2s - 2s^2 - s -1)/2 ] = (1/c)[ (s - 2s^2 -1)/2 ] = (1/(2c))(s - 2s^2 -1). So O-H = (1/2 - s, (s - 2s^2 -1)/(2c)). Note that 1/2 - s = -(s - 1/2). And s - 2s^2 -1 = -(2s^2 - s +1) = -(2s^2 - s +1). So we can write:

O-H = ( - (s - 1/2), - (2s^2 - s +1)/(2c) ). Actually, s - 2s^2 -1 = -(2s^2 - s +1). So O-H = (1/2 - s, -(2s^2 - s +1)/(2c)). Good.

Now compute cross product (O-H) × v. Since we are in 2D, cross product scalar = (O-H)_x * v_y - (O-H)_y * v_x. Compute v = (v_x, v_y) = ((2s-1)(s+1), -c(1+2s)). Note that (2s-1)(s+1) = D = (2s-1)(s+1). And c = √(1-s^2). We'll keep.

So compute:

(O-H)_x * v_y = (1/2 - s) * ( -c(1+2s) ) = -(1/2 - s) c(1+2s) = (s - 1/2) c(1+2s).
(O-H)_y * v_x = [ -(2s^2 - s +1)/(2c) ] * [ (2s-1)(s+1) ] = -(2s^2 - s +1)/(2c) * D. So (O-H)_y * v_x = -(2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c). Then cross = (O-H)_x v_y - (O-H)_y v_x = (s-1/2)c(1+2s) - [ -(2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c) ] = (s-1/2)c(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c).

Now, note that D = (2s-1)(s+1). And (s-1/2) = (2s-1)/2. So (s-1/2)c(1+2s) = ((2s-1)/2) c (1+2s). So cross = ((2s-1)c(1+2s))/2 + (2s^2 - s +1) D/(2c). Put over common denominator 2c: = (1/(2c))[ (2s-1)c^2(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D ]. Because (2s-1)c(1+2s) * c? Actually, first term: ((2s-1)c(1+2s))/2 = ((2s-1)c(1+2s))/2. Multiply numerator and denominator to combine with second term? Better: cross = 1/(2) [ (2s-1)c(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D/c ]. Write as (1/(2c))[ (2s-1)c^2(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D ].

Now compute the bracket B = (2s-1)c^2(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D. Since c^2 = 1-s^2, we have (2s-1)(1-s^2)(1+2s) + (2s^2 - s +1) D. And D = (2s-1)(s+1). So we can factor (2s-1) maybe. Let's compute:

First term: (2s-1)(1-s^2)(1+2s). Second term: (2s^2 - s +1) (2s-1)(s+1) = (2s-1)(s+1)(2s^2 - s +1). So B = (2s-1)[ (1-s^2)(1+2s) + (s+1)(2s^2 - s +1) ].

Now compute the inner sum: (1-s^2)(1+2s) + (s+1)(2s^2 - s +1). Expand:

(1-s^2)(1+2s) = (1)(1+2s) - s^2(1+2s)=1+2s - s^2 -2s^3.
(s+1)(2s^2 - s +1) = (s)(2s^2 - s +1) + 1*(2s^2 - s +1)=2s^3 - s^2 + s +2s^2 - s +1 = 2s^3 + s^2 + 0s +1? Let's do: 2s^3 - s^2 + s +2s^2 - s +1 = 2s^3 + (-s^2+2s^2)=+s^2, and s - s =0, so +1. So sum = 2s^3 + s^2 + 1. Then total inner = (1+2s - s^2 -2s^3) + (2s^3 + s^2 +1) = 1+2s - s^2 -2s^3 +2s^3 + s^2 +1 = 2 + 2s. So inner = 2(1+s). Thus B = (2s-1)*2(1+s)=2(2s-1)(1+s). So cross = (1/(2c)) * B = (1/(2c)) * 2(2s-1)(1+s) = (2s-1)(1+s)/c.

Now, |v| = PA = √(2(1+s)). So distance from O to L = |cross|/|v| = |(2s-1)(1+s)/c| / √(2(1+s)) = (2s-1)(1+s)/(c) * 1/√(2(1+s)) = (2s-1)√(1+s)/(c√2). Because (1+s)/√(1+s)=√(1+s). So distance = (2s-1)√(1+s)/(c√2).

Now compute radius R_circ = distance from O to B. O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)). B = (1-2s^2, -2s c). Compute squared distance:

dx = 1-2s^2 - 1/2 = 1/2 - 2s^2.
dy = -2s c + (s+1)/(2c) = (1/(2c))( -4s c^2 + s+1 ) = (1/(2c))( -4s(1-s^2) + s+1 ) = (1/(2c))( -4s +4s^3 + s+1 ) = (1/(2c))( -3s +4s^3 +1 ) = (1/(2c))(1 -3s +4s^3). So R_circ^2 = (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + [ (1 -3s +4s^3)/(2c) ]^2 = (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2/(4c^2). We need to show that this equals the square of the distance from O to L: d^2 = (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2). Because d = (2s-1)√(1+s)/(c√2) => d^2 = (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2).

So we need to verify that:

(1/2 - 2s^2)^2 + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2/(4c^2) = (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2).

We can compute (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 = (1/4) - 2s^2 + 4s^4? Actually, (1/2 - 2s^2)^2 = 1/4 - 2*(1/2)*(2s^2) + 4s^4 = 1/4 - 2s^2 + 4s^4. So left side = 1/4 - 2s^2 + 4s^4 + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2/(4c^2). Multiply both sides by 4c^2 to clear denominators? Let's do.

Left side * 4c^2 = 4c^2(1/4 - 2s^2 + 4s^4) + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2 = c^2(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4) + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2.

Right side * 4c^2 = 4c^2 * (2s-1)^2 (1+s)/(2 c^2) = 2 (2s-1)^2 (1+s). So we need to show:

c^2(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4) + (1 -3s +4s^3)^2 = 2 (2s-1)^2 (1+s).

Now, c^2 = 1-s^2. So compute c^2(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4) = (1-s^2)(1 - 8s^2 + 16s^4). Expand: = (1)(1-8s^2+16s^4) - s^2(1-8s^2+16s^4) = 1 -8s^2+16s^4 - s^2 +8s^4 -16s^6 = 1 -9s^2 +24s^4 -16s^6.

Now compute (1 -3s +4s^3)^2 = 1 +9s^2 +16s^6 -6s +8s^3 -24s^4? Let's do carefully: (a+b+c)^2 with a=1, b=-3s, c=4s^3. Then a^2=1, b^2=9s^2, c^2=16s^6, 2ab=2*1*(-3s)=-6s, 2ac=2*1*4s^3=8s^3, 2bc=2*(-3s)*(4s^3)=-24s^4. So sum = 1 -6s +9s^2 +8s^3 -24s^4 +16s^6. Then total left = (1 -9s^2 +24s^4 -16s^6) + (1 -6s +9s^2 +8s^3 -24s^4 +16s^6) = combine: constants: 1+1=2; s term: -6s; s^2: -9s^2+9s^2=0; s^3: 8s^3; s^4: 24s^4-24s^4=0; s^6: -16s^6+16s^6=0. So left = 2 -6s +8s^3 = 2(1 -3s +4s^3). So left = 2(1-3s+4s^3). Right side: 2(2s-1)^2(1+s) = 2( (2s-1)^2 (1+s) ). Now, (2s-1)^2 = 4s^2 -4s +1. So (2s-1)^2 (1+s) = (4s^2-4s+1)(1+s) = (4s^2-4s+1)*1 + (4s^2-4s+1)*s = 4s^2-4s+1 +4s^3-4s^2+s = 4s^2-4s+1+4s^3-4s^2+s = 4s^3 -3s +1. So right = 2(4s^3 -3s +1) = 2(1 -3s +4s^3). That matches left. So identity holds. Thus distance from O to L equals radius. Therefore L is tangent to circumcircle of triangle B P F.

But we can also try to redo the proof with general r and see if the same simplifications hold. Let's attempt to carry out the proof with general r.

We have M=(0,0), N=(1,0). Let r and R be radii with r<R and R-r < 1 < R+r (intersection). Then:

C = (-r,0)
D = (1+R,0)
A = (x0, y0) with x0 = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, y0 = √(r^2-x0^2) >0.
B = (x0, -y0)

Now, P = circumcenter of triangle A, C, D. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (C+D)/2 = (-r + 1+R)/2 = (1+R-r)/2 = X. So P = (X, y_P). And y_P is determined by PA = PC (or PA = PD). Compute PC^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2. PA^2 = (X - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2. Set equal:

(X - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2 = (X + r)^2 + y_P^2. Expand:

X^2 -2X x0 + x0^2 + y_P^2 -2y_P y0 + y0^2 = X^2 +2X r + r^2 + y_P^2. Cancel X^2,y_P^2:

-2X x0 + x0^2 -2y_P y0 + y0^2 = 2X r + r^2.

Now, x0^2+y0^2 = r^2. So x0^2+y0^2 - r^2 =0. So we have:

-2X x0 -2y_P y0 = 2X r + r^2 - r^2 = 2X r. Wait: Bring terms: -2X x0 + x0^2 -2y_P y0 + y0^2 = 2X r + r^2. Since x0^2+y0^2 = r^2, then left = -2X x0 -2y_P y0 + r^2. So equation: -2X x0 -2y_P y0 + r^2 = 2X r + r^2 => -2X x0 -2y_P y0 = 2X r => Divide by -2: X x0 + y_P y0 = -X r. So y_P y0 = -X(x0+r). Thus y_P = -X(x0+r)/y0. That is our earlier expression.

Now, we also have from geometry: X = (1+R-r)/2. And x0 = (1+r^2-R^2)/2. Then x0+r = (1+r^2-R^2 + 2r)/2 = (1 + 2r + r^2 - R^2)/2 = ((1+r)^2 - R^2)/2 = ((1+r-R)(1+r+R))/2. So y_P = -X * ((1+r-R)(1+r+R)/2) / y0. And y0 = √(r^2-x0^2). We can also express y0 in terms of r,R.

Now, we need line AP. We can compute its direction v = P-A. Then we need F = second intersection of line AP with Γ. Using power of P: PN^2 - R^2 = ? Compute PN^2 = (1-X)^2 + y_P^2. Given the difficulty, maybe the problem has a known solution using properties of the Euler line and the nine-point circle. Since H is orthocenter of PMN, and P is circumcenter of ACD, perhaps there is a homothety that maps triangle PMN to triangle ABC? Let's explore.

There is a known configuration: The circumcenter of triangle ACD is the midpoint of the segment connecting the centers of the two circles? Let's test with numeric: For r=0.5,R=1.3,d=1, we had P=(0.9,-1.684). The midpoint of MN is (0.5,0). Not P. So no.

But maybe P is the reflection of the midpoint of MN across the line through A? Not.

Another approach: Use complex numbers with M=0, N=1, and keep r as a parameter. Then we can express all points as functions of r and R. Then we can compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF and show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent. This will be an algebraic identity in r and R. The algebra might be heavy but we can try to simplify by using the substitution that we used for r=1. Since we have a proof for r=1, we could try to see if the general case can be reduced to that by a scaling of the configuration? Actually, we can scale the entire figure by a factor λ, which changes all lengths by λ. This scaling is a similarity transformation. Under this, the property we want to prove is invariant. So if we take any configuration, we can scale it so that the radius of Ω becomes 1 (i.e., set r=1). But then the distance MN becomes d/r. So we have a configuration where Ω is unit circle, but MN is some distance d'. Now, can we apply a rotation or translation to make MN=1? That would be another scaling, which would also change r. So we cannot have both r=1 and MN=1 unless d=r. So the case we proved is only for those configurations where the distance between centers equals the radius of Ω. But is that a restriction? Actually, we can choose a different scaling: scale the whole figure so that MN=1. Then r becomes r/d. That is a general parameter. So we need to prove for all r (positive) with MN=1. Our proof for r=1 is a special case. However, perhaps we can argue that the problem is symmetric in the sense that if we swap the two circles, the condition changes? Not.

Maybe we can use a similarity transformation that sends the circle Ω to the unit circle and also sends the point N to (1,0). That would set r=1 and d=1 (since N at (1,0) implies MN=1). But that is only possible if the original distance between centers equals the original radius of Ω. So that is a special case. But we can also apply a rotation that sends the line MN to the real axis, and then apply a scaling that sends the distance MN to 1, but then r becomes r/d. So the parameter r is free. So we need to prove the statement for all r.

Given the algebraic success with the special case, perhaps we can attempt to carry out the same coordinate computation for general r (MN=1) and see if the algebra simplifies. Let's try that.

We have M=(0,0), N=(1,0). Let r and R be radii with r<R and R-r <1 < R+r. Define:

Let s = R - r, t = R + r. Then r = (t-s)/2, R = (t+s)/2. We have s<1<t.

We have:

y_P = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)).

Now, we need to find F, the second intersection of line AP with Γ. Since A is on Γ, and P is on line AP, we can param: A + u v, with v as above. So u=0 (A) and u = -2 (A-N)·v / |v|^2. So u_F = -2 (A-N)·v / |v|^2.

We can compute (A-N)·v. N=(1,0). A=(x0, y0). So A-N = (x0-1, y0). Compute dot with v.

We have v = ((1+t)/2) w, so (A-N)·v = ((1+t)/2) (A-N)·w. And |v|^2 = ((1+t)^2/4) |w|^2. So u_F = -2 * ((1+t)/2) (A-N)·w / ( ((1+t)^2/4) |w|^2 ) = -2 * ((1+t)/2) * (4/(1+t)^2) * (A-N)·w / |w|^2 = -2 * (2/(1+t)) * (A-N)·w / |w|^2 = -4/(1+t) * (A-N)·w / |w|^2. So u_F = -4/(1+t) * (A-N)·w / |w|^2.

That is fine.

Thus u_F = 2(s+t)/(1+t) * 1/|w|^2 = 2(s+t)/(1+t) * (t^2-1)/(t^2-s^2). But t^2-s^2 = (t-s)(t+s) = (2r)(2R?) Actually, t-s=2r, t+s=2R, so t^2-s^2=4rR. Thus F = A + u_F v = A + ((t-1)/r) * v. And v = ((1+t)/2) w, so F = A + ((t-1)/r)*((1+t)/2) w = A + ((t-1)(1+t))/(2r) w = A + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) w. Since w = ( s, - (t √(1-s^2))/√(t^2-1) ), we have F = A + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) w.

Given the algebraic success with the special case, and the fact that we now have a clean expression for u_F = (t-1)/r, which is a nice simplification, maybe we can continue to compute O for the general case using the same technique we used for the special case. Alternatively, compute F_x = x0 + (t^2-1)s/(t-s). Since x0 = (1-st)/2, so F_x = (1-st)/2 + (t^2-1)s/(t-s). Combine over denominator 2(t-s): = [ (1-st)(t-s) + 2s(t^2-1) ] / [2(t-s)]. So = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s. Factor t? Not. Maybe we can simplify using the relation that t^2-1 =? Not.

Given the algebra is getting heavy, but perhaps we can find a simpler parameterization. Note that we have a free parameter r and R. The condition that circles intersect is that there exist two intersection points. That is equivalent to the existence of a point A on both circles. In our coordinate system, we can set A = (a, b) with a = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, b = √(r^2-a^2). That is fine.

Now, perhaps we can use the fact that the configuration is determined by the two circles, and the points C and D are on the line of centers. There is a known lemma: The circle through B, E, F is orthogonal to the circle with diameter MN? Or maybe the line through H parallel to AP is the radical axis of the circles with diameters AP and something.

Another synthetic idea: Since H is orthocenter of triangle PMN, we have that the reflections of H across the sides lie on the circumcircle. In particular, the reflection of H across MN is on circumcircle of PMN. That point is H' = (X, -y_H) if we assume MN is x-axis. And we already know that H' lies on circumcircle of PMN. Also, note that P is on the circumcircle of PMN? No, P is a vertex, so it's on the circle. So the circle (PMN) contains P, M, N, H'. Now, what is the relationship between this circle and the circle (BEF)? Perhaps they are tangent? Or something.

We have that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). In our coordinate proof for the special case, we found that the distance from O to L equals radius. That came from a simplification where O = (1/2, - (s+1)/(2c)) and L had direction v. In the general case, we might expect that O = (1/2, something) and that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent. Let's try to see if we can find O in the general case using the same method as before. We can set up equations for O = (Ox, Oy) such that distances to B, P, F are equal. That will give two linear equations (since the constant terms cancel if we subtract appropriately). Let's attempt that.

We have:

B = (x0, -y0)
P = (X, y_P)
F = (F_x, F_y)

We want:

|O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2   (1)
|O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2   (2)

From (1): (Ox - x0)^2 + (Oy + y0)^2 = (Ox - X)^2 + (Oy - y_P)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2Ox x0 + x0^2 + Oy^2 +2Oy y0 + y0^2 = Ox^2 -2Ox X + X^2 + Oy^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2.

Cancel Ox^2, Oy^2:

-2Ox x0 + x0^2 +2Oy y0 + y0^2 = -2Ox X + X^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2.

Bring terms:

-2Ox x0 +2Ox X +2Oy y0 +2Oy y_P + x0^2 + y0^2 - X^2 - y_P^2 =0 => 2Ox (X - x0) + 2Oy (y0 + y_P) + (x0^2+y0^2 - X^2 - y_P^2)=0.

Now, x0^2+y0^2 = r^2. And X^2+y_P^2 =? X^2+y_P^2 = ( (1+s)/2 )^2 + ( (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) )^2 = (1+s)^2/4 + (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(4(t^2-1)) = (1/4)[ (1+s)^2 + (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

But note that (1+s)^2 = 1+2s+s^2. And (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(t^2-1) = (1+t)^2(1-s^2)/(t^2-1). Not obviously simplifying to something like? However, we might compute the constant term K = r^2 - X^2 - y_P^2. That is not zero generally. So equation (1) becomes:

2Ox (X-x0) + 2Oy (y0 + y_P) + K = 0. (1)

Now from (2): (Ox - X)^2 + (Oy - y_P)^2 = (Ox - F_x)^2 + (Oy - F_y)^2. Expand:

Ox^2 -2Ox X + X^2 + Oy^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2 = Ox^2 -2Ox F_x + F_x^2 + Oy^2 -2Oy F_y + F_y^2.

Cancel Ox^2,Oy^2:

-2Ox X + X^2 -2Oy y_P + y_P^2 = -2Ox F_x + F_x^2 -2Oy F_y + F_y^2.

Bring terms:

-2Ox X +2Ox F_x -2Oy y_P +2Oy F_y + X^2 + y_P^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2 =0 => 2Ox (F_x - X) + 2Oy (F_y - y_P) + (X^2+y_P^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2)=0. (2)

Thus we have two linear equations in Ox, Oy. If we can show that these equations are consistent and that the solution (Ox, Oy) lies on the vertical line x=1/2, and that the distance from O to line L equals the radius, then we are done. But the algebra is heavy.

Given the time, and the fact that we have a proof for the special case where the distance between centers equals the radius of Ω (i.e., d=r), we need to see if that case is actually sufficient. Could it be that the problem statement implies that the radius of Ω is less than the radius of Γ, but there is no condition that d=r. However, we can apply a similarity transformation that sends Ω to the unit circle and also sends the line MN to the real axis. That is always possible. But then the distance between centers becomes d' = d/r. So we have a configuration where r=1, and MN = d' (some number). In our proof, we assumed MN=1 and r=1, which forced d'=1. So that is only a special case. However, note that the problem is invariant under scaling (uniform scaling). So if we take any configuration, we can scale it so that the distance MN becomes 1. That sets r' = r/d. So we have a configuration with MN=1 and r = r/d. That is a general parameter. So we need to prove the statement for all r (positive) with MN=1. Our proof for r=1 is a special case. But we can try to see if the same algebraic simplification we used for r=1 can be extended to general r. In our proof for r=1, we used the fact that r=1 to get P on Ω and that A = e^{iθ} (on unit circle). In the general case, we have r arbitrary. However, we can use a different coordinate normalization: set the circle Ω to be the unit circle (r=1) and then let the distance MN be d (not necessarily 1). That is also a valid normalization. In that normalization, we have M=0, N=d, Ω: |z|=1, and we derived expressions for points in terms of d and θ. We can then attempt to compute O and verify tangency. This might be simpler because we have r=1. Let's try that.

Set M=(0,0), N=(d,0), with d>0. Let Ω: |z|=1. Let A = (cosθ, sinθ) with θ∈(0,π) (since B is symmetric). Then B = (cosθ, -sinθ). The condition that A lies on Γ: |A-d| = R. So R^2 = (cosθ-d)^2+ sin^2θ = 1+d^2-2d cosθ. That's fine. Then C = (-1,0) (on Ω left of M). D = (d+R,0) (since order C-M-N-D). So X = (C+D)/2 = (-1+d+R)/2 = (d+R-1)/2. Then P = (X, y_P) with y_P from PA=PC. Compute: PC^2 = (X+1)^2 + y_P^2. PA^2 = (X - cosθ)^2 + (y_P - sinθ)^2. Setting equal gives: (X - cosθ)^2 + (y_P - sinθ)^2 = (X+1)^2 + y_P^2. So F_y = (1/2)√(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) - (t √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1))/(2r) = √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) ( 1/2 - t/(2r) ) = √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) * ( (r - t)/(2r) ). Since r = (t-s)/2, then r - t = (t-s)/2 - t = (t-s-2t)/2 = (-t-s)/2 = -(t+s)/2. So (r-t)/(2r) = -(t+s)/(4r). Thus F_y = - √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) * (t+s)/(4r). But t+s = 2R, so F_y = - (2R)/(4r) √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) = - (R/(2r)) √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2). And note that √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) = √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). So F_y = - (R/(2r)) √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). That is a nice expression.

Now, F_x = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(2r). Write r = (t-s)/2, so 2r = t-s. So ((t^2-1)s)/(2r) = ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). So F_x = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). Combine: = [ (1-st)(t-s) + 2s(t^2-1) ] / [2(t-s)]. Expand numerator: (1-st)(t-s) = (t-s) - st(t-s) = t-s - s t^2 + s^2 t. Then add 2s(t^2-1) = 2s t^2 - 2s. Sum = t-s - s t^2 + s^2 t + 2s t^2 - 2s = t-s + s t^2 + s^2 t - 2s = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - s - 2s? Actually, -s - 2s = -3s. So numerator = t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s. So F_x = (t + s t^2 + s^2 t - 3s) / [2(t-s)]. That's messy. But maybe we can also express F_x in terms of A and something. Note that A_x = (1-st)/2, so F_x = A_x + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). That is fine.

Now, we also have P_x = (1+s)/2, P_y = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). And B = ( (1-st)/2, - (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ). So B_x = A_x, B_y = - A_y.

Now, we need H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. With M=(0,0), N=(1,0), P as above. We already derived y_H = - s (1-s)/c? Wait, that was for r=1 case. In general, we need to compute H. Using the same method as before: altitude from P is vertical line x = P_x = (1+s)/2. Altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. PN: from P to N = (1 - P_x, -P_y) = (1 - (1+s)/2, -P_y) = ((2 - (1+s))/2, -P_y) = ((1-s)/2, -P_y). So slope of PN = (-P_y)/((1-s)/2) = -2P_y/(1-s). Perpendicular slope = (1-s)/(2P_y) (since negative reciprocal). So line through M with that slope: y = ((1-s)/(2P_y)) x. Intersection with x = P_x gives y_H = ((1-s)/(2P_y)) * P_x = ((1-s)/(2P_y)) * ((1+s)/2) = (1-s^2)/(4P_y). Now, P_y is negative, so y_H is negative? Actually, 1-s^2>0, P_y negative, so y_H negative. So H = (P_x, y_H) = ((1+s)/2, (1-s^2)/(4P_y)). Now, we need to compute P_y: P_y = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). So 4P_y = - 2(1+t)√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1). Then y_H = (1-s^2) / ( - 2(1+t)√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = - (1-s^2) √(t^2-1) / (2(1+t)√(1-s^2)) = - √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1) / (2(1+t)) because (1-s^2)/√(1-s^2)=√(1-s^2). So y_H = - √((1-s^2)(t^2-1)) / (2(1+t)). Note that √((1-s^2)(t^2-1)) = √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)). And 1+t is positive. So H = ( (1+s)/2, - √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) / (2(1+t)) ). That is a nice expression.

Now, the line L through H parallel to AP. Direction v = P-A. We have A = ( (1-st)/2, (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ). P = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) ). So v = ( (1+s)/2 - (1-st)/2, - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) - (1/2)√((t^2-1)(1-s^2)) ) = ( (1+s -1 + st)/2, - (1/2)[ (1+t)√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) + √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) ] ) = ( (s + st)/2, - (√(1-s^2)/2)[ (1+t)/√(t^2-1) + √(t^2-1) ] ) = ( s(1+t)/2, - (√(1-s^2)/2)[ (1+t + (t^2-1))/√(t^2-1) ] ) = ( s(1+t)/2, - (√(1-s^2)/2)[ (t^2+t)/√(t^2-1) ] ) = ( s(1+t)/2, - (√(1-s^2) t(1+t))/(2√(t^2-1)) ). So v = (1+t)/2 * ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = (1+t)/2 w, where w = ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ). So v is proportional to w.

Thus line L: (x,y) = H + λ w, λ real.

Now, we need to show that L is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. That is, the distance from the center O of circle (BEF) to L equals the radius. We can try to compute O using the linear equations we derived earlier, but now with the general expressions. That might be doable.

Given the time, I think we should attempt to compute O using the fact that we have expressions for B, P, F in terms of s,t. We'll use the notation:

Let’s define:

U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). Note that U = √((t^2-1)(1-s^2)).

Then we have:

B = ( (1-st)/2, -U/2 )
P = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) ) = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) )
F = ( (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s), - (R/(2r)) U ) with R = (t+s)/2, r = (t-s)/2, so R/(2r) = (t+s)/(2*(t-s))? Actually, R/(2r) = ((t+s)/2) / (2*(t-s)/2) = (t+s)/(2(t-s)). So F_y = - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) * U.

Now, note that B_y = -U/2, so F_y is something like -U*(t+s)/(2(t-s)). And P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). But we can also write P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) = - (1+t) / (2) * √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = - (1+t)/(2) * (√(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1)). Not directly U.

Now, we need to compute O. We can set up the equations:

From |O-B|^2 = |O-P|^2:
(O_x - B_x)^2 + (O_y - B_y)^2 = (O_x - P_x)^2 + (O_y - P_y)^2.
Expand: (O_x^2 -2O_x B_x + B_x^2) + (O_y^2 -2O_y B_y + B_y^2) = (O_x^2 -2O_x P_x + P_x^2) + (O_y^2 -2O_y P_y + P_y^2). Cancel O_x^2,O_y^2:
-2O_x B_x -2O_y B_y + B_x^2+B_y^2 = -2O_x P_x -2O_y P_y + P_x^2+P_y^2.
Bring terms:
2O_x (P_x - B_x) + 2O_y (P_y - B_y) + (B_x^2+B_y^2 - P_x^2 - P_y^2) = 0. (Eq1)

Similarly, from |O-P|^2 = |O-F|^2:
2O_x (F_x - P_x) + 2O_y (F_y - P_y) + (P_x^2+P_y^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2) = 0. (Eq2)

We can compute the constants.

First, compute B_x = (1-st)/2, P_x = (1+s)/2, so P_x - B_x = (1+s - (1-st))/2 = (s + st)/2 = s(1+t)/2 = s(1+t)/2.

Now, B_y = -U/2, P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). Let's compute P_y - B_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) + U/2 = (1/2)[ U - (1+t) √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ].
But U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). So U - (1+t) √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = √(1-s^2)[ √(t^2-1) - (1+t)/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t^2-1 - (1+t))/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t^2-1 -1 - t)/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t^2 - t -2)/√(t^2-1) ] = √(1-s^2)[ (t-2)(t+1)/√(t^2-1) ]? Because t^2 - t -2 = (t-2)(t+1). Yes. So P_y - B_y = (1/2) √(1-s^2) * (t-2)(t+1)/√(t^2-1) = (1/2) √(1-s^2) (t-2)(t+1)/√(t^2-1). That is not obviously simplifying.

Now, compute the constant term K1 = B_x^2+B_y^2 - P_x^2 - P_y^2. B_x^2+B_y^2 = ( (1-st)^2/4 + U^2/4 ) = ( (1-st)^2 + U^2 )/4. P_x^2+P_y^2 = ( (1+s)^2/4 + (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(4(t^2-1)) ) = ( (1+s)^2 + (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) )/4. So K1 = (1/4)[ (1-st)^2 + U^2 - (1+s)^2 - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

Now, U^2 = (t^2-1)(1-s^2). So (1-st)^2 + U^2 = (1 - 2st + s^2 t^2) + (t^2-1)(1-s^2) = 1 - 2st + s^2 t^2 + (t^2-1) - (t^2-1)s^2 = 1 - 2st + s^2 t^2 + t^2 -1 - s^2 t^2 + s^2 = (1-1) + t^2 - 2st + s^2 = t^2 - 2st + s^2 = (t-s)^2. So (1-st)^2+U^2 = (t-s)^2. Then K1 = (1/4)[ (t-s)^2 - (1+s)^2 - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

Now, (t-s)^2 - (1+s)^2 = (t^2 -2st + s^2) - (1+2s+s^2) = t^2 -2st + s^2 -1 -2s - s^2 = t^2 -2st -1 -2s = (t^2 -1) - 2s(t+1). So K1 = (1/4)[ (t^2-1) - 2s(t+1) - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

Now, (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) = (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1). So K1 = (1/4)[ (t^2-1) - 2s(t+1) - (1+t)^2 (1-s^2)/(t^2-1) ].

This is not zero generally. So Eq1 becomes:

2O_x * (s(1+t)/2) + 2O_y * (P_y - B_y) + K1 = 0 => O_x s(1+t) + 2O_y (P_y - B_y) + K1 = 0. (Eq1')

Now, compute Eq2: 2O_x (F_x - P_x) + 2O_y (F_y - P_y) + (P_x^2+P_y^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2) = 0.

We need F_x - P_x and F_y - P_y. Compute F_x = A_x + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). A_x = (1-st)/2. So F_x = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). P_x = (1+s)/2. So F_x - P_x = (1-st)/2 - (1+s)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s) = ( -2s)/2? Actually, (1-st -1 - s)/2 = (-s - st)/2 = -s(1+t)/2. So F_x - P_x = -s(1+t)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s) = s[ - (1+t)/2 + (t^2-1)/(t-s) ].

Simplify the bracket: - (1+t)/2 + (t^2-1)/(t-s) = - (1+t)/2 + (t-1)(t+1)/(t-s). Write (t^2-1)/(t-s) = (t-1)(t+1)/(t-s). So bracket = (t+1)[ -1/2 + (t-1)/(t-s) ] = (t+1)[ ( - (t-s) + 2(t-1) ) / (2(t-s)) ] = (t+1)[ (-t+s+2t-2) / (2(t-s)) ] = (t+1)[ (t + s -2) / (2(t-s)) ].

Thus F_x - P_x = s (t+1) (t+s-2) / (2(t-s)). So that is one.

Now, F_y - P_y. We have F_y = - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) * U. P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). But U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). So F_y = - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) * √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). P_y = - (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)). So F_y - P_y = - (t+s) √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2) / (2(t-s)) + (1+t) √(1-s^2) / (2 √(t^2-1)) = √(1-s^2)/2 [ - (t+s) √(t^2-1)/(t-s) + (1+t)/√(t^2-1) ].

Simplify the bracket: = [ - (t+s) (t^2-1) + (1+t)(t-s) ] / [ (t-s) √(t^2-1) ]. Compute numerator: - (t+s)(t^2-1) + (1+t)(t-s) = - (t+s)(t^2-1) + (1+t)(t-s). Expand (1+t)(t-s)= (1+t)t - (1+t)s = t + t^2 - s - st. And - (t+s)(t^2-1)= - (t+s)(t^2-1) = - (t+s)(t^2-1). Write t^2-1 = (t-1)(t+1). So we have: = - (t+s)(t^2-1) + t + t^2 - s - st. Factor maybe? Not.

Alternatively, note that we can also compute F_y - P_y using the expression for v and the param. Then u_F - 1 = (2(t-1) - (t-s))/(t-s) = (2t-2 - t + s)/(t-s) = (t + s -2)/(t-s). And v_y = - (√(1-s^2) t(1+t))/(2√(t^2-1)). So F_y - P_y = (t+s-2)/(t-s) * ( - (√(1-s^2) t(1+t))/(2√(t^2-1)) ) = - (t+s-2) t √(1-s^2) / (2(t-s) √(t^2-1)). That is a nice expression. Let's verify if this matches the previous. Multiply numerator and denominator: = - t √(1-s^2) (t+s-2) / (2(t-s) √(t^2-1)). Yes.

So we have:

F_x - P_x = s (t+1)(t+s-2) / (2(t-s))
F_y - P_y = - t √(1-s^2) (t+s-2) / (2(t-s) √(t^2-1))

Now, we also need the constant term K2 = P_x^2+P_y^2 - F_x^2 - F_y^2. That is messy. But perhaps we can find that Eq2 simplifies nicely when we use the expressions for O_x and O_y from Eq1'. We also have that A is on both circles, so |A-M| = r and |A-N| = R. So we have:

|A - λ(A+C)| = |C - λ(A+C)| = r. This gives r^2 = (1-λ)^2 + λ^2 - 2λ(1-λ) Re(A\bar{C}). Similarly, |A - μ(A+D)| = |D - μ(A+D)| = R.

Now, also collinearity: Since C, M, N, D are collinear, the points C, M, N, D lie on a line. This line is not necessarily through the origin. But we know that M = λ(A+C) and N = μ(A+D). |1 - λ(1+C)|^2 = |1 - λ - λC|^2 = (1-λ)^2 + λ^2 - 2λ(1-λ) Re(C). Since Re(C)=cosα. Similarly, |C - λ(1+C)|^2 = |C - λ - λC|^2 = |(1-λ)C - λ|^2 = (1-λ)^2 + λ^2 - 2λ(1-λ) Re(C). So they are equal automatically. So r^2 = (1-λ)^2 + λ^2 - 2λ(1-λ) cosα.

Perhaps we can use inversion about point A with radius such that the circles become lines, and then use properties of the orthocenter of triangle formed by the images of M and N. Let's try that more concretely.

Now, the circumcenter O = (Ox, Oy) can be found. Since the y-coordinates are all proportional to U, we can write O_y = -U * k for some k. Then the condition that O is equidistant from B, P, F gives:

|Ox - B_x|^2 + (U k - U/2)^2 = |Ox - P_x|^2 + (U k + U (1+t)/(2(t^2-1)))^2? Actually, careful: O_y = -U k, B_y = -U/2, so O_y - B_y = -U k + U/2 = U(1/2 - k). |O-B|^2 = (Ox - B_x)^2 + U^2 (1/2 - k)^2
|O-P|^2 = (Ox - P_x)^2 + U^2 ( (1+t)/(2(t^2-1)) - k)^2
|O-F|^2 = (Ox - F_x)^2 + U^2 ( (t+s)/(2(t-s)) - k)^2

We need these to be equal. So we have two equations. The unknowns are Ox and k. This might be easier because we can subtract equations to eliminate the (Ox - ...)^2 terms.

(Ox - B_x)^2 - (Ox - P_x)^2 + U^2[(1/2 - k)^2 - ((1+t)/(2(t^2-1)) - k)^2] = 0

Let's attempt to compute O using the formula with points B, P, F. Since we have coordinates, we can compute the determinant for Ox and Oy. Let's denote:

B = (B_x, B_y)

We'll compute:

D = 2(B_x(P_y - F_y) + P_x(F_y - B_y) + F_x(B_y - P_y)) (denominator for both Ox and Oy).
Then:

Ox = ( (B_x^2+B_y^2)(P_y - F_y) + (P_x^2+P_y^2)(F_y - B_y) + (F_x^2+F_y^2)(B_y - P_y) ) / D
Oy = ( (B_x^2+B_y^2)(P_x - F_x) + (P_x^2+P_y^2)(F_x - B_x) + (F_x^2+F_y^2)(B_x - P_x) ) / D

Now, we have expressions for B, P, F in terms of s,t,U. Note that U = √(t^2-1) √(1-s^2). Also, we have r = (t-s)/2, R = (t+s)/2. We'll use these to simplify.

First, compute B_x = (1 - s t)/2, B_y = -U/2.
P_x = (1+s)/2, P_y = - (1+t)U/(2(t^2-1))? Because √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = U/(t^2-1). So P_y = - (1+t)U/(2(t^2-1)). Let's denote A = (1+t)/(t^2-1). Then P_y = -U A/2.
F_x = B_x + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s) = (1-st)/2 + ((t^2-1)s)/(t-s). F_y = - (t+s)U/(2(t-s)). Let's denote B = (t+s)/(t-s). Then F_y = -U B/2.

Now, note that P_y - F_y = -U A/2 + U B/2 = U/2 (B - A). So P_y - F_y = U/2 (B - A). Also, F_y - B_y = -U B/2 + U/2 = U/2 (1 - B). And B_y - P_y = -U/2 + U A/2 = U/2 (A - 1). So these are linear in U.

Now, also compute squares:

B_x^2+B_y^2 = ( (1-st)^2/4 + U^2/4 ) = ( (1-st)^2 + U^2 )/4. But we already computed (1-st)^2+U^2 = (t-s)^2. So B_x^2+B_y^2 = (t-s)^2/4.

P_x^2+P_y^2 = ( (1+s)^2/4 + U^2 A^2/4 ) = ( (1+s)^2 + U^2 A^2 )/4. And U^2 = (t^2-1)(1-s^2). So P_x^2+P_y^2 = ( (1+s)^2 + (t^2-1)(1-s^2)A^2 )/4.

F_x^2+F_y^2 = (F_x^2 + U^2 B^2/4). We'll need that.

Now, the numerator for Ox: Nx = (B_x^2+B_y^2)(P_y - F_y) + (P_x^2+P_y^2)(F_y - B_y) + (F_x^2+F_y^2)(B_y - P_y). Substituting the expressions:

Nx = ((t-s)^2/4) * (U/2 (B-A)) + (P_x^2+P_y^2) * (U/2 (1-B)) + (F_x^2+F_y^2) * (U/2 (A-1)).

Factor U/2: Nx = (U/2)[ ((t-s)^2/4)(B-A) + (P_x^2+P_y^2)(1-B) + (F_x^2+F_y^2)(A-1) ].

Similarly, denominator D = 2( B_x(P_y - F_y) + P_x(F_y - B_y) + F_x(B_y - P_y) ). Compute each:

B_x(P_y - F_y) = B_x * (U/2 (B-A)) = (1-st)/2 * U/2 (B-A) = U(1-st)(B-A)/4.
P_x(F_y - B_y) = P_x * (U/2 (1-B)) = (1+s)/2 * U/2 (1-B) = U(1+s)(1-B)/4.
F_x(B_y - P_y) = F_x * (U/2 (A-1)) = U F_x (A-1)/2.

So sum = U/4 [ (1-st)(B-A) + (1+s)(1-B) + 2F_x (A-1) ]? Because U F_x (A-1)/2 = (U/4)*2F_x (A-1). So denominator D = 2 * (U/4) [ (1-st)(B-A) + (1+s)(1-B) + 2F_x (A-1) ] = (U/2)[ (1-st)(B-A) + (1+s)(1-B) + 2F_x (A-1) ].

Thus D = (U/2) * S, where S = (1-st)(B-A) + (1+s)(1-B) + 2F_x (A-1).

Now, Nx = (U/2) * T, where T = ((t-s)^2/4)(B-A) + (P_x^2+P_y^2)(1-B) + (F_x^2+F_y^2)(A-1). So Ox = Nx / D = (U/2 T) / (U/2 S) = T/S.

Thus Ox = T/S, independent of U. Similarly, we can compute Oy = N_y / D, where N_y = (B_x^2+B_y^2)(P_x - F_x) + (P_x^2+P_y^2)(F_x - B_x) + (F_x^2+F_y^2)(B_x - P_x). And D = (U/2)S. Then Oy = (U/2 * something) / (U/2 S) = something / S. So O = (Ox, Oy) = (T/S, something/S). So O is expressed as a rational function of s,t and F_x, etc. This is still heavy, but note that T and S are linear in F_x? Actually, T contains terms with P_x^2+P_y^2 and F_x^2+F_y^2, which are quadratic in F_x. So S and T are polynomials in s,t,F_x.

Given the time, I think it's too heavy
</think>
